Behind the Curtain

by Cassie_Bones

Summary

Agatha is the star of the show. Rio is the lurker backstage. Lilia is their Drama teacher. When she forces Rio to audition for the play as "extra credit" for her class, sparks begin to fly as Rio and Agatha are cast as romantic leads in their high school play. What happens next?

Title credit goes to Discord User Cantaloupe

Notes

High School Drama Club AU. These bitches are gay AND dramatic. Enjoy!

See the end of the work for more notes

Chapter 1: Extra Credit

Chapter One

Agatha Harkness was a straight-A student. She studied hard in her classes, worked tirelessly for her 4.0 GPA, and participated in class, sometimes to her own detriment. It wasn’t her fault that she read the source material more closely than her AP Lit teacher and was able to correct him on Celie’s motivations in The Color Purple.

She loved that book.

She also took part in school-run clubs.

Well, okay, at least one club. But it was arguably the most important to her.

Westview High’s drama club was the best in New Jersey - at least according to Agatha and anybody with any common sense (also according to Agatha). And Agatha was the star of the show…opposite Jen Kale, at least.

Agatha hated Jen Kale.

Jen Kale hated Agatha.

They had a good thing going there.

Too bad they both vied for the spotlight.

Lilia Calderu, their director and theater teacher, was often stalked by the two them, especially leading up to auditions, the both of them trying to pry info of the next show practically from her brain, asking her endless questions and, frankly, annoying the hell out of her. Lilia had taken to locking herself in her classroom with the lights off when she had no students to teach. If any of the admin wanted to talk to her at that time, they either had to send her an email or fuck all the way off until she was in the company of her students.

Lilia thought she was in the clear one morning in early January, sitting under her desk to sneakily eat her lunch as she queued up a “slime tutorial” on her phone, not minding how ridiculous it was that she had to do this to get away from two of the most annoying fucking students…

“Lilia?”

The voice hit her like a bullet and she jumped, cursing under her breath as she hit her head under the desk.

“Are you okay?” The voice was calm, almost bored in the level of unconcerned it emitted. Lilia peeked her head over the edge of the desk, her brow furrowing at an unusual sight.

“Rio?” she asked. “What are you doing in my classroom? How are you in my classroom?” Rio pointed at the window, through which a cold gust of Jersey air was blowing in, ruffling the stacks of papers she’d piled on the counter. “Through the window?” she huffed. “Really?”

“You said you wanted to talk to me at lunch,” Rio said with a shrug. “I knew you weren’t gonna answer the door. Not with Agatha and Jen stalking you right now.” She bit back her grin at the sight of her kooky old teacher crouched behind the desk. “The window seemed like the best option.”

“How did you even get up here?” Lilia asked, getting to her feet with a grunt as her old bones protested. “We’re on the second floor.” There was a door from her classroom that led straight into the balcony of the auditorium, where she often had students act out their scenes, making sure that they were projecting enough for her to hear all the way in the back.

“I made vines,” Rio said, holding out a hand. In her fingerless-gloved palm sat a little, bright green vine. “I’m a green witch, apparently.”

“That’s not surprising,” Lilia said, picking up her lunch off her chair, which she’d been using as a table, and setting it on her desk. “You’ve always been kind of one with nature.”

Rio shrugged. “Nature’s cool,” she said, nonchalantly, but Lilia knew that there was passion there. Like Agatha Harkness, Rio Vidal was one of her more hardworking students. She presented a tough front, always dressed in black, ripped clothing, a little dirt or soot adding to the disheveled look, and thick-lined eyes, but she could hold her own in a dramatic study.

Too bad she didn’t really inject that same passion into her scene-acting.

“Sit down,” Lilia said. “You got lunch? Go ahead and eat. I’m going to.”

Lilia picked up her sandwich again as Rio opened her backpack and pulled out an apple and a bag of Doritos. Lilia snorted and shook her head at the meager lunch, but she had potato chips on her sandwich so, really, who was she to judge?

“So,” Rio said, after biting into her apple, “what did you need to see me for? Did I not hand in some assignment on time?”

“No, your analysis of Shakespeare’s theft of intellectual property was fantastic. The man really was a bastard.” Rio snorted at that. “That’s not why I invited you to lunch.” She cleared her throat. “We have to talk about your scenes.”

Rio groaned, rolling her eyes. “I’m not an actor, Lilia,” she huffed. “I do tech. I paint sets and move shit around. I don’t act.”

“But you need to do more than just stand there, staring at a piece of paper the entire time,” Lilia huffed. “Come on! Where’s your passion?”

“Backstage where I left it,” Rio huffed, ripping open the bag of Doritos. “Leave all the acting to Agatha and Jen.”

“Shh,” Lilia said, eyeing the door. The blinds were down, but she could practically feel their eyes on her. “You’ll summon them,” she whispered, paranoid.

Rio cackled, shaking her head as she bit into a chip. “You are a kook,” she said.

“And you are in danger of receiving your first B in my class,” Lilia said.  Rio frowned, her gaze turning serious. “That’s gonna bring down your grade point average, isn’t it?” she asked, smugly, taking a big bite of her sandwich.

“I’ve done all the work,” Rio argued. “I read my lines and I write my papers…I should get the same A I’ve always gotten. You’re really going to ruin my shot at Valedictorian over a stupid monologue?”

“Passion and Memorization are two key factors when it comes to a good scene reading,” Lilia said. “It’s part of the rubric I give you after every reading. You’ve never been able to fully memorize a single thing, nor have you been able to inject any amount of passion into your readings. If you want that A, you have to really earn it. Work as hard in my class as you do in…botany. I know you love that one.”

“It’s AP Earth Science, actually,” Rio muttered, “but whatever.” She inhaled deeply through her nose. “Fine,” she said. “What’s my next assignment gonna be?”

Lilia grinned at that, somewhat evilly, as she reached out and stole a chip from the bag in front of Rio. “You,” she said, munching on the chip, “are going to audition for the Spring Musical.”

Agatha was bored out of her mind in AP Latin. She’d already conjugated all the verbs and translated all the texts the teacher had given them for the day’s assignment. She’d been learning Latin practically since the moment she learned how to speak, her mother reciting it like most moms probably recite prayers or bedtime stories. 

Agatha glanced around the room, watching as her classmates bent over their desks, brows furrowed, sweat dripping down their foreheads, and snorted. Amateurs . None of them had what it took to use actual witchcraft, to become so attuned to magic and the world around them to do anything useful with the sparks between their fingers.

There was a buzz in her pocket and she jumped slightly, raising her arms above her head and arching her back to make it look like she was just stretching. Nobody cared.

Agatha reached for her phone, keeping one eye on the teacher, who was using his own phone, and saw a text. From Rio. She felt her face flush.

Rio Vidal was…Agatha didn’t really know what they were to each other. They’d joined Drama as Freshman together, but Rio was more inclined to set design whereas Agatha preferred the spotlight. Rio was always there to help her run lines, though. 

Literally, whenever Agatha asked, no matter where they were or what Rio was doing, she would drop everything to help her rehearse her lines. Agatha appreciated that. 

What she didn’t appreciate was the staring during rehearsals. She could always feel Rio’s gaze on her, but whenever she looked over, it was as if Rio had just decided that something else warranted her attention, something more important than meeting Agatha’s eye.

They sat together at lunch sometimes, when Agatha wasn’t borderline stalking the Drama director, Lilia Calderu. They ate with a group of other drama kids, including Jen Kale, Agatha’s nemesis…okay, yeah, that was dramatic. But Agatha didn’t like her. She was always trying to one-up Agatha in Drama. They were constantly fighting over the lead role in plays, constantly nagging Lilia about who got to play which part. The poor woman was probably sick of them.

Jen and Agatha sat at opposite ends of the long cafeteria table. Rio usually sat right across from Agatha, though, making jokes or going over scenes for Agatha’s upcoming auditions. They also talked about their classes and magic. Rio often made flowers grow in her hands, from nothing but seeds, a little bit of soil, and a splash of water. She always offered them to Agatha, which made the other girl feel…warm.

Rio was odd, but smart. She was strange, but beautiful in a way. She wore heavy eyeliner and carried a switchblade in her back pocket. She got straight A’s and wore mostly black, large rips in the knees of her jeans, slouchy beanies over her raven black hair, dark green eye shadow and nearly black lipstick. She smirked a lot, like there was always a joke in her ear. It was disarming.

Agatha’s heart did a stupid little flip in her chest when she saw Rio’s name pop up on her screen. The other girl had been absent for lunch. Agatha and Jen had spent half the period walking around, looking for Lilia, anyway. But Agatha had noticed her absence, nonetheless.

Rio: U busy?

Agatha looked around the room at all the others struggling to decipher the dead language. There were still twenty minutes left in class. Agatha was only half-paying attention to the book of runes she’d stolen from her mother’s study. She knew them by heart already.

Agatha: Not in the slightest.

Agatha: What’s up?

Agatha: Why weren’t you at lunch?

Agatha: Did the teacher’s pet get detention?

Rio: ha no

Rio: my mom would kill me

Rio: I need your help

Rio: it’s urgent

Rio: meet me in the bathroom

Agatha: which one?

Rio: Languages 

Rio: that’s where u r, rite?

Agatha: yeah, but how did you know that?

Rio: ;-)

Stalker , Agatha thought as she slipped her phone back into her pocket and raised her hand. It took a moment for the Latin teacher to glance up and see her. He only even did so when she cleared her throat.

“Yes, Miss Harkness?” Mr. Herb sighed, lifting one brow at her. “What can I do for you? Did you find a spelling error in the text? Again.”

“Not this time,” Agatha said, standing up. “I just have to use the lady’s room. That time of the month again, you know?”

Mr. Herb wrinkled his nose. “Too much info, Harkness,” he said. “Just take your pass and go deal with your lady problems. Don’t be out too long.”

Agatha grabbed the bathroom pass (in this case, a large stick that was nearly as tall as she was) and made her way to the bathroom three doors away, the stick hung over one shoulder.

The second she made it inside, she saw Rio, lounging on the windowsill, the window open, letting in the frigid January air. Agatha leaned the bathroom stick against the wall just as Rio turned to her, giving her that lopsided smirk in greeting. Agatha felt her cheeks warm, flipping her hair a bit over her shoulder.

“So,” she said, “what’s up?”

Rio held out her fist to Agatha, opening it to reveal a daisy.

“M’lady,” she said, holding the flower out to Agatha. Agatha flushed, plucking the flower from her fingertips.

“Is this what the urgency was all about?” Agatha huffed, twirling the flower between her fingers. “A daisy? You’re losing your touch, Vidal.”

“If you don’t want it,” Rio said, giving Agatha a bored look, “give it back.”

“No,” Agatha said, holding the daisy to her chest, protectively, careful not to crush it. “But, seriously, what did you want? I know it wasn’t just to give me another flower.” She tucked it into her bun and felt a thrill when Rio’s smile visibly softened for a fraction of a second.

“I need your help with an assignment,” Rio said.

“ You need my help?” Agatha scoffed. “Aren’t you a straight-A student?”

“Not for long,” Rio retorted, somewhat bitterly. “Not if Lilia has anything to say about it. She threatened to give me a B if I don’t ‘put the work in’ on this next assignment.” Rio used air quotes, her short, black-painted nails curling in the air. She looked very annoyed.

Agatha felt something.

“Wow,” she said. “Pretty ballsy of Lilia to give the golden girl a B,” she said. “How will your GPA ever recover?”

“I have a 4.2,” Rio replied, casually. Agatha’s jaw dropped.

“How is that even possible?”

“I do a lot of extra credit,” Rio informed her, with a shrug. “I also have a photographic memory, which helps.”

“I’ll bet,” Agatha muttered. “You still aiming for Valedictorian? Even with a B from Lilia, you’ll still get it.”

“That’s not the point,” Rio huffed. At Agatha’s raised eyebrows, she relented. “Okay, it’s kind of the point. But I’ve also never gotten a B in any class all throughout high school, okay? I put in the work, I study my ass off, and I’m going to get into a good school and graduate with honors. I’m a goddamn green witch.” To demonstrate, she made more flowers grow in her palm, green sparks flying out. “I’m not going to let some stupid audition ruin my chances.”

“That’s a bit dramatic, don’t you think?” Agatha asked, crossing her arms over her chest.

“Said the queen of the drama club,” Rio retorted. “Aren’t you trying to get into Julliard to live out your dreams of…what? Starring as Elphaba in Wicked ?”

“Ha ha,” Agatha said, dryly. “I’m double majoring, actually. Theater and Spellcraft.”

“Spellcraft?” Rio asked. “Like…you want to compose new spells? That’s hard, isn’t it?”

“It is the hardest thing to learn. Even harder to execute. So much can go wrong if you even get the smallest thing wrong. It can all explode in your face. Literally.” Agatha jumped up on the window ledge next to Rio, smiling, fondly, as she gazed out into space, Rio watching her with interest.

“And that’s what you want to do with your life?” she asked. “Sounds risky.”

Agatha glanced sideways at her, grinning. “What’s life without a bit of risk?” Rio nodded, conceding. “Anyway, what, exactly, is it that you want my help with? Running lines?” Her eyes widened. “Oh my god, did Lilia tell you what the spring musical is gonna be? You have to tell me, as your best friend.”

“You’re my best friend?” Rio asked, her cheeks going a little pink even as she raised a brow, doubtful.

“Well, duh!” Agatha said, pointing at the flower in her hair. “What else would we be if not the very best of friends?”

“How many flowers have you given me ?” Rio retorted, tilting her head.

“That’s not fair,” Agatha huffed. “I’m a purple witch. I cannot bring flowers to life in my hand.”

“Well then,” Rio said, “what do you have to offer your best friend ?” She gave Agatha a toothy grin, tilting her head again.

“I can help you with your audition,” Agatha said. “Like you help me with my lines all the time. I can help you blow the cardigan off Lilia’s frail old Medieval body. If , of course, you tell me what the next musical is.”

Rio leaned in, so close that her lips brushed Agatha’s ear, inhaling the scent of lavender and vanilla. Agatha shivered. Rio’s lips quirked up in a smirk “I have no idea what it is,” she whispered, laughing as Agatha groaned and pushed her away, starting back toward the door. She reached for Agatha’s arm, pulling her back. “Wait,” she said. “Come on, I seriously need your help, though. Please, Agatha?”

Agatha rolled her eyes, turning back to her. Rio’s eyes were warm and soft, the deepest brown she’d ever seen; fathomless. There was real desperation there, though Rio tried to mask it. There was hope in the usually aloof girl’s eyes. She was actually worried about the possibility of getting a B, of ruining her straight-A streak.

It was ridiculous, Agatha knew, but she could relate. She, too, would do anything to keep her grades the way that they were. She, too, would do anything to perfect a monologue or a scene.

She groaned, acquiescing. “Fine,” she said, her breath freezing in her lungs as Rio pulled her into a tight squeeze, her arms going around Agatha’s shoulders, face pressed into the crook of Rio’s neck. Her face felt instantly hot and she immediately pushed her way out of Rio’s arms. 

The other girl let go immediately, looking embarrassed, rubbing the back of her neck. “Sorry,” Rio said, her cheeks pink. “Um, thanks. This means a lot.”

“Yeah, well,” Agatha said, “what are best friends for?” Rio smiled softly at her then looked down. “So, did she assign you a monologue or what?”

“No, she said I had to pick my own. I am not as familiar with theater shit as I probably should be, being a techie and all. Any suggestions?” Agatha opened her mouth. “No Shakespeare.” 

Agatha closed her mouth, thinking. “I have a whole binder of monologues at home,” she said. “If you want to come over after school, we can look at them together.” She said it casually, but her heart was pounding in her chest. She’d never really invited anybody over to her house before. She’d never considered herself close enough to anybody before. Not close enough for hangouts at each other’s houses.

Rio was silently staring at her. Agatha cleared her throat.

“Or,” she said, looking down, “I could just bring it in tomorrow. Yeah, I’ll just bring in the binder tomorrow.” She started to turn away when she realized that Rio was still holding her wrist. Her sudden move seemed to break her from whatever trance she was in.

“Right,” Rio said. “No. I mean, yeah. I’ll, uh, I’ll come over. After school. We can do homework there, too, right? I don’t want to fall behind just looking over monologues.”

Agatha nodded, swallowing hard. “Sure,” she said. “Of course. So, uh, I’ll meet you by the front steps? Two-thirty?”

“Sounds good,” Rio said with that same lopsided grin that she always wore.

Agatha returned it and started to leave again, but she couldn’t.

“Uh, Rio?” she said, looking down at where Rio still had a firm grasp on her wrist.

“Hmm?” Rio looked down and her eyes widened as she pulled her hand away like it had been burned. “Sorry,” she said, clearing her throat. “Um, I’ll see you at two-thirty.”

“Yeah,” Agatha said, grabbing the bathroom stick and heading toward the door, not looking back. “Bye.”

By the time she made it to class, the bell had started to ring. Agatha grabbed her things from her desk, ignoring the look of disappointment from the teacher as she placed the stick back in its rightful place, and ran out of the classroom to make it to her next class on time. 

Her wrist burned for the entire rest of the schoolday. 

Chapter 2: Revelations

Chapter Summary

Rio goes to Agatha to find a monologue for her audition and ends up finding something more than she thought she would.

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Chapter Two

Rio Vidal exuded mystery. Or, at least, she tried to exude mystery. She was a quiet kid who turned into an equally quiet teenager. She liked to read and work in the garden with her abuelita. She learned how to mow the lawn when she was eleven years old, barely strong enough to push the thing across the yard, but she’d done it. Her papa had been proud, giving her a thumbs up from the porch as she’d beamed at him.

She did see him mowing over the spots she’d missed later on, but that was okay. It was her first time, but it wouldn’t be her last time.

Before long, Rio would be the only one mowing the lawn.

He had passed when she was fourteen, just before her junior high graduation, leaving her alone with her grieving mother, Marisol. Marisol Vidal had dressed all in black for that entire summer, her grief shrouding her in a dark cloud. Rio had joined her, feeling as though the color drained from both of their lives.

Eventually, Marisol went back to dressing in bright colors, doing her makeup and her hair, leaving the house with a bright smile, kissing her daughter goodbye, telling her how much she loved her…she was back to her old self.

Rio didn’t remember who her old self was, though. She was just…dark. 

Marisol didn’t mind. She helped Rio get rid of all the old clothes from her wardrobe that didn’t fit her anymore, either in size or personality. She took Rio on a back to school shopping spree, not judging when the only things that Rio really wanted to buy were in shades of gray, black, and the darkest greens because, despite her moody teen goth phase, green would always be her favorite color.

She was a green witch, after all. Just like her papa.

When she saw Agatha their first day of high school, dressed in varying shades of purple, hair curling wildly around her head, and an unamused expression on her face as she fought with Jen Kale about something that was probably very important (but Rio couldn’t hear it), she felt her entire body go hot and suddenly the black all over her body was a little suffocating.

Agatha was snarky and sharp-tongued and quick-witted and somewhat cruel, but she was also so…beautiful. Rio’s breath froze in her lungs the first time Agatha’s gaze met hers, her eyebrows lifting briefly in interest before she looked away. 

Rio felt as though she were having a heart attack at the time, only it just felt…good. There was a thrill that ran through her, like electricity running up her fingertips after an electric shock. She was startled by her physical reaction to this… girl . Agatha was just a girl.

But, no, because Rio had seen other girls. She had met other girls. She had kissed other girls. In junior high, she’d held hands with other girls in the hallways, shared nervous, shy glances with girls at camp, and even brought a couple home.

After her father died, however…

She hadn’t felt this way for anybody since the grief took over her heart, stilling it with mourning. She’d honestly thought she would never feel like that again.

And then Agatha Harkness let out a cackle in the middle of the hallway, her head tilted back, curly hair falling down her back, hand over her chest in her mirth. Rio didn’t hear the joke that was made before that, but the sound of Agatha’s laughter made her own lips quirk up for the first time in forever.

It almost hurt to smile again.

She and Agatha had two classes together that year: Honors English 9 and Drama I. The former was pretty uneventful. Rio spent much of her time glancing at Agatha in between answering questions on grammar and whatever novel they were reading and writing her analytical essays. Agatha spent that same time arguing with the teacher on all those same things.

In Drama, though, Agatha thrived . And Rio loved to watch it. Agatha’s monologues were riveting, Rio leaning forward in her chair to hear her whispers during particularly emotional scenes. Agatha cried real tears, laughing genuinely, and blew the roof off the auditorium.

Rio was sure, within only three months of knowing the other girl, that she was in love. But she wasn’t about to admit it.

Not even when she followed Agatha to auditions in ninth grade and sat all the way in the back just so she could watch and clap for Agatha’s monologue from Maleficent . She commanded the audience’s attention, owned the stage, stalked across it slowly, languidly, like a cat…

At one point, it felt as though Agatha was looking only at Rio, speaking directly to her as she said, “I like you begging; do it again,” with that cruel grin of hers. She had even dressed the part that day, in a long, purple coat that swished when she walked back and forth across the stage and her hair fashioned back in a severe bun, her cheekbones outlined and shaded.

She was Maleficent that day.

Which is probably why Lilia had casted her as the Witch in their production of Into the Woods . Jen had been casted as The Baker’s Wife, who died part way through the play, quite tragically.

Oh well.

Rio had chosen to join the drama club that day, after seeing Agatha’s audition, before even knowing what it all entailed. She refused to audition for any part, big or small, too uncomfortable with the thought of thousands of eyes on her at any given time. 

Instead, she opted for tech crew, building sets with the other ‘lurkers’ as Jen Kale so kindly called them. This way, she could still be close to Agatha without actually putting herself out there. It was better this way.

She even continued to take Drama as a class, though she couldn’t have cared less about it if she tried. Her dad had taught her more than just how to mow the land, after all. He’d taught her how to hold and use a hammer, screwdriver, paintbrush…you name it. Even at fourteen years old, Rio was one of the most skilled techies on the crew.

She wondered, sometimes, if being a lesbian added to her home improvement skills. It would be kind of funny if it did.

Drama wasn’t harder than any of Rio’s other classes, anyhow. She liked reading plays as much as she liked reading books. Understanding the symbolism behind their words was easy, especially for somebody with a pretty good grasp on the English language and a college reading level. It was always the acting that fucked her up.

Rio did not like it when people looked at her.

Well, most people.

She’d been cast as a tree in The Wizard of Oz third grade school play because she’d frozen during her audition, her face going alarmingly pale, her brown eyes never blinking as she looked out at the auditorium, where sat only two people. She would never have made it as a munchkin or anything else, let alone Scarecrow or Dorothy. 

And she was fine with it.

It became a problem when her grades were on the line, though. Rio was pretty comfortable with Lilia, who refused to let any of the students call her Miss Calderu because it made her sound so serious and she hated that. So they were on a first name basis despite the objections from her colleagues. Lilia was cool to talk to. She was kooky and funny and talked to her students like they were her equals, not her lessers. She had a way of putting them all at ease, at making them laugh during serious moments, of locking eyes with them during a monologue to make them breathe more easily.

Rio begged to just do her monologues in front of Lilia, rather than in front of the whole class like was typically required. Lilia had just given her a look, not speaking, as she twisted up her lips, eyebrows raised. Rio had been near tears, but even that didn’t move her.

“I can’t go giving you special treatment, Vidal,” she’d said. “How would that make me look?”

“Like the best teacher in history?” Rio replied, hopefully. “Like my favorite teacher?”

Lilia scoffed. “Not a chance,” she said. “Either you do the monologue in front of the class or I give you a B.”

Rio had almost taken the B the first time, so desperate to get out of this torture.

But she relented, performing - if you could call it that - a monologue from a play called “Serendipity and Serenity” that Lilia had suggested. She had a photographic memory, had memorized every line and bit of punctuation on the paper as soon as she’d looked at it for the second time. She could have done that monologue in her sleep.

But when she felt everybody’s eyes on her on that stage…she felt her body go cold, her tongue sitting thick in her mouth as she stumbled through the words, mumbling and clearing her throat and just…

She was bad at acting.

But Agatha Harkness was good at acting. Very good. She aced every single assignment, showing up dressed as close to her characters as she could get, putting on makeup and accents alike. She moved about the stage like an Ivy League-educated master of her craft, the confidence exuding from her every pore. She looked directly at her audience, crumpling the script in her hand and tossing it over her shoulder

Every.

Single.

Time.

She was made for this.

Which is exactly why Rio had asked for her help to prepare for the “extra credit” audition. In front of the whole drama club. Including techies and the girl who practically single-handedly ran the music section, Alice Wu Gulliver.

It felt as though there was something forming in the pit of Rio’s stomach as she waited for Agatha to show up after school, checking the time on her phone, again and again. It felt like time had slowed down to a crawl. It couldn’t still be 2:29. 

It had been, like, five whole minutes.

“I’m here!” 

Agatha’s voice nearly made Rio tip over in surprise. She spun around, feeling her face flush even as snow fell around them. Agatha lifted one eyebrow in mirth, one side of her mouth quirking up.

“Did I scare you?” she asked, her voice tinged with fake sweetness.

Rio scowled at her, painted on her usual unaffected aura. “No,” she huffed. “I just…didn’t expect you to be so annoyingly on time.”

“I was early, actually,” Agatha admitted. “I have a free period for ninth.”

“So do I,” Rio said. She used to have art, but the teacher didn’t appreciate every art project she made being around death and decay. After the third “zombie” painting she made (which Rio thought was actually quite tasteful, with flowers and other vegetation growing from the zombie’s many orifices and wounds) she was taken out of studio art, promised a passing grade if she simply never returned. She took it, using the free period to work on her homework and other projects.

She still drew zombies, but now it was just in her own time.

“I didn’t know that,” Agatha said, thoughtfully. “We could have left school a lot earlier if I had.”

Rio shrugged. “Next time,” she said before she could think better of it. She watched as Agatha’s cheeks turned pink beneath her scarf, but that could have just been a consequence of the icy wind that was blowing on her face. Her nose was also pretty pink. Her blue eyes were nearly covered by a matching beanie, her hair coming out of it in a mess of curls.

“You took your hair down,” Rio commented, swallowing thickly.

“Oh, yeah,” Agatha said. “My hair tie broke.” She shrugged. “Is that all you’re going to wear?”

While Agatha was in a full winter coat that went down to her knees, her matching scarf and hat, and a pair of thick woolen mittens, Rio was wearing little more than her plaid flannel over her black “The Road” t-shirt and ripped jeans. Her combat boots were pretty warm, at least. And she had on a beanie.

“This is pretty much it,” she said with a shrug. “I don’t get cold very easily.”

“Doesn’t mean you can’t catch a cold,” Agatha huffed, unwinding her scarf from her neck as Rio watched, brows furrowed in confusion. Before she could protest, Agatha had looped the scarf around her neck, twice, tightening it a little so that it covered Rio’s nose, then started for the sidewalk. “Come on,” she said, without looking at Rio, who was now definitely warmer, though that had little to do with the scarf around her neck.

She swallowed thickly as she followed Agatha down the school path to the sidewalk. She matched Agatha’s step and pace but said nothing as they walked, the snow falling around them, muting everything as it covered the ground in a soft, white sheet.

Rio watched as Agatha’s breath puffed out every now and then, her boots making soft crunching noises, her curls fluttering behind her. She was dressed today in her usual purple. She wore a royal purple cowl-neck sweater with burgundy buttons covering most of her neck, and a pair of dark blue boot-cut jeans with a pair of brown leather boots over them. The boots were pretty worn and well-loved, a bit of wool spilling over the top around her ankles. 

Agatha, who loved to dress up for her auditions, tended to dress casually for every other day, Rio noticed. She had an impressive collection of sweaters, blouses, and even some t-shirts that she cycled through at school. She tended to prefer jeans or different-colored (usually purple) trousers, and she rarely wore skirts, but, when she did, they were about knee-length and matched whatever shirt she was wearing. Her boots were worn mostly during the colder months, but Rio knew that she also had a few pairs of patterned high-tops that would show up in fall and winter, weather-permitting. 

And, of course, she wore character shoes for rehearsals when they were warranted. 

But, all in all, Agatha had an impressive wardrobe. Rio could remember exactly what she wore on any given day, thanks to her photographic memory, though she didn’t consider herself a lover of nice clothing. Her wardrobe consisted of a lot of flannel, a ton of ripped jeans, one or two nice dresses for special events, a suit in dark green and black for formal occasions, and a lot of band shirts. Her mother loved supplying the latter, laying them on Rio’s bed sometimes as a ‘special treat’ after school. Whatever shirt Marisol gifted her, Rio wore it the next day at school, kissing her mama goodbye as she beamed at her. 

“We’re here,” Agatha said, breaking through Rio’s thoughts as they stopped in front of a rather large, two-story house. The porch wrapped all the way around the picture-perfect white house. There was an attached garage up the driveway, which Agatha led Rio toward, stamping the snow off their boots as she typed in the code to open the door to the side of the garage.

They stepped into a little room a couple steps down from the kitchen, a couple of hooks hanging by the door. When Agatha saw that they were empty, she let out a little sigh of relief. “She’s not home,” she whispered to herself.

“Who isn’t?” Rio asked. 

Agatha jumped, as if she’d forgotten that somebody had followed her home. “Um, nobody,” she said, shaking her head. Snowflakes, half-melted, fell from her head. “Doesn’t matter. Come on.” She motioned for Rio to take off her shoes, then follow her. They walked through the kitchen to another hallway, across which Rio could see a sitting room that looked like something out of a catalog, every piece of furniture pristine and in its place. Almost sterile.

“Your parents are very…organized,” she commented as they started to stomp up the carpeted steps in their socks. 

“Parent,” Agatha corrected. “It’s just my mother and me.”

“Oh,” Rio said. “Sorry. Yeah, that’s…it’s just me and my mom, as well.”

“It’s difficult, huh?” Agatha asked with a wry smile as they reached the upstairs hallway. 

“It can be,” Rio agreed, following her to the end of the hall to a door with a cross nailed to it. She hadn’t pegged Agatha for the religious type, but she didn’t comment on it. “After my dad died, things got kind of…rough.”

Agatha’s hand froze on the doorknob to her bedroom. She turned around, eyes wide. “Shit,” she said. “I had no idea. I’m so sorry. I didn’t…I meant that moms can be difficult. My mom is…a lot. I didn’t know about your dad.” She was giving Rio that sympathetic look that she hated , but she didn’t tell her that.

She just shrugged, nonchalantly. “It’s been four years,” she said. “Almost. I’m…fine.” She had no better word for it. That was the truth. She was fine. It had taken a long time for her to get to the point where she could say that honestly. “I’d rather not talk about it. Why don’t we talk about your dad, instead?”

“I don’t have a dad,” Agatha said, rolling her eyes. “At least I don’t have one according to my mother.” She pushed open the door to her room and Rio took a moment to absorb it all.

The room was tidy, the walls painted plum. A full-size bed sat beneath a window that looked out into the backyard. There were Broadway posters all around the room and a slanted wall to the left, presumably where the roof was. Another window to the right, underneath which sat a desk, cluttered with papers and books. The floor was hardwood but covered with a plaid-patterned rug. Her bedspread was floral, teal and lavender flowers dotting a black background. There was a stuffed bunny by the headboard. The headboard itself was a shelf, with a few books and trinkets lining it. Above their heads, purple fairy lights lined the walls and hung down from the ceiling. Agatha flipped a switch and they started to flicker as she smiled up at them.

Her closet had a folding door, which was open, her impressive collection of clothing hanging up, with more clothes folded on the above shelf, shoes sticking out underneath. There was another dresser against the foot of her bed. Agatha padded over to it and opened the bottom drawer while Rio removed the scarf from around her neck and placed it on the rolling desk chair.

“Gimme a minute,” Agatha said, brushing past her and walking into a different room to the left of her bedroom. Rio saw a flash of tile, so she guessed that it was a bathroom. She nodded, though Agatha didn’t see it.

Rio continued to look around the room, thumbing through some of the books that littered the various surfaces, like the shelves that somebody had mounted along the walls. Most of Agatha’s books were plays, but there were some novels that Rio recognized from their English classes at school. She picked through them, laughing as she stopped at one in particular, plucking it from the shelf. It had so many notes sticking out of it, she was certain that Agatha had read it probably over a dozen times.

She heard the bathroom door open and grinned. “You know,” she said, smartly, “ The Color Purple isn’t actually about the color…” she turned around, her face feeling hot when she saw Agatha - usually dressed impeccably in her blouses or sweaters and designer jeans - wearing a pair of sleep shorts that went down to her knees and a too-big t-shirt that said Gypsy on it on big, bold letters. Her hair was up again, the daisy Rio had given to her earlier once again tucked in her hairband. She hadn’t wiped her makeup off, but she’d donned a pair of purple slippers.

“I know exactly what that book is about, thank you very much,” Agatha said. “I’ve read it so many times, I have it committed to memory. I’ve also seen the show on Broadway.”

“Of course you have,” Rio chuckled, coming back to herself. She replaced the book on Agatha’s shelf, making sure it went right back where Agatha had originally had it. She watched Agatha out of the corner of her eye, though, as she placed her school clothes in a hamper in the corner. “What makes it your favorite?” Rio asked, glancing back to the bookshelf just as Agatha looked back at her.

“Haven’t you read it?” Agatha asked, pulling up the comforter on her bed and ducking underneath.

“Of course I have,” Rio huffed. “It’s required reading. Surprising, though, considering the subject material.”

“That’s kind of why I love it,” Agatha admitted, hefting a box filled with plays and folders onto her bed. “Okay, so this is every monologue I’ve ever done or thought of doing for an audition or class assignment. Some of them may not be appropriate for a high school audition, though. I’ve filed them based on where they’re from. These,” she said, holding up a Mickey Mouse folder, “are all Disney monologues. Probably the most appropriate, but also somewhat cheesy. These,” she said, “are lesser-known, but still brilliant, in my humble opinion. They are separated by gender. Blue tabs are for guys, purple tabs are for ladies.”

“Do you think Lilia cares if I read for a masculine role?” Rio asked.

“Not in the slightest. She’s always reminding us how she used to have to bind and dress like a guy so that she could perform on the stage as a woman.”

“One of the original drag queens,” Rio joked. Agatha laughed and Rio felt a thrill go through her at the sound. “So I can just leaf through these?” she asked.

“Be my guest,” Agatha said, standing up and walking over to her desk to pull something off the shelf above it. “I’m doing a monologue from…you know what, I’m not even gonna tell you. This isn’t about me. This is about you.” She took a deep breath, placing her book into a drawer on her desk. “Did you find anything that you like?”

Rio plucked a paper with a blue tab, her eyes scanning over it for a moment, widening with every word she read. She placed it to the side, picking up another monologue, also blue-tabbed. She frowned and placed it back in the piled, then plucked a purple-tabbed monologue. She read it, shrugged, placed it to the side atop the other one. She did this with one or two more, before holding them up for Agatha to review. 

“These are good,” Agatha hummed, reading through them as she sat back in her chair, one knee tucked up against her chest while the other rocked her back and forth. She got to the first blue-tabbed monologue that Rio had picked up, a slow smile spreading over her lips. “This one,” she said, definitively. “This one is so good. I mean, it’s for a guy, but…are you, like…” She flipped her hand in front of her with a pout. 

Rio wrinkled her nose. “Am I what ?”

“You know…” Agatha did the move again. Rio’s brows knotted together. Agatha rolled her eyes. “You know what, if you don’t understand, I’m just gonna assume you’re not and move one.”

“Are you asking me if I’m gay?” Rio asked, laughing.

“Well, obviously ,” she huffed. 

“How do go from this ,” Rio made the exaggerated hand movement, “to gay ?”

“It’s a common hand movement,” Agatha said. “Also, stop saying that so loud! My mom could be home at any second.”

“Oh, is she super religious?”

“For a witch, yes,” Agatha huffed. “And very homophobic. She doesn’t even like me to mention that word.” She padded to the bedroom door, shutting it. 

“Wow,” Rio said, shaking her head. “My mom is kind of the exact opposite of that.” She cleared her throat and put on a big smile. “‘ Mija! Did you do anything GAY today? Did you see any cute GAY girls today? Did you find me a new nuera?’ ”

“Oh my god, that’s hilarious,” Agatha laughed, shaking her head. “My mother would have a heart attack if she even thought I liked girls.”

“Well,” Rio said, tilting her head at her, “ do you?”

Agatha frowned at her, crossing her arms over her chest. “I asked you first.”

“I thought it was pretty well known,” Rio said, “I’ve been out since junior high. I’ve dated several girls.”

“You have?” Agatha asked. Rio didn’t miss the way her cheeks pinkened.

“Not since before my dad passed away,” Rio admitted. “But yeah…I figured everybody knew. It’s not like I’ve ever had a boyfriend. Boys are actually pretty terrified of me, to be honest.” 

“I think most people are a little terrified of you,” Agatha pointed out.

“Not you,” Rio replied, her voice probably too soft and open for the casualty of this conversation. 

Agatha was quiet as she shot Rio a smile at her over her shoulder. Then she reached into her desk drawer again, pulling something small out. She tossed it on the bed in front of the other girl. Rio looked down, her cheeks warming at the sight of a little braided bracelet on the bed.

A bracelet with the lesbian pride flag colors on it. She looked up at Agatha, who shrugged, blushing.

“My mom sent me to Bible Camp,” she said, “for three years running. I made friends with some other… interesting girls. Kind of fell in love with one, too.” She took a deep breath. “It didn’t work out, obviously, but she taught me about that flag, those colors. My mother still doesn’t know what they mean, so I can’t hang up a pride flag, but I can wear that on occasion.” She shrugged. 

“I’m sorry about that,” Rio said. “I hope that one day you can be authentically yourself, in all aspects.” 

Agatha shot her a grateful smile, and Rio plucked the bracelet from the bed, readying to toss it back to Agatha, but Agatha held up her hand.

“Keep it,” she said, pulling three more out of her desk drawer. “I have plenty. Besides, you’ve given me plenty of gifts.” She pointed to the flower sticking out of her ponytail. “Might as well pay back the favor.”

Rio let out a laugh at that, slipping the bracelet onto her left wrist. “Thanks,” she said. “My mom is gonna flip when she sees this. She’s been begging me to put more color into my wardrobe for years .” 

“Wait until she finds out the significance,” Agatha said.

“Oh God, I will never hear the end of it,” Rio groaned, burying her face in her hands.

“Your mom sounds great,” Agatha said. “Can’t relate.” Rio’s eyes softened as she looked back up at her, but Agatha waved her off. “I’m fine,” she assured her. “I’m gonna go to Juilliard or some Spellcraft super college and leave Westview and my mother in my dust.”

“Good plan,” Rio said, fingering the threads of the bracelet on her wrist. “So, um, which monologue did you say I should do? One that needs me to be gay?”

“You don’t need to be gay for this one,” Agatha huffed, walking toward her. “Really, you just need to not be a coward to do this one as a woman.” She handed Rio the monologue and Rio’s face flushed when she saw which one it was. 

“This one?” she asked. “Really?”

Agatha nodded. “I think you could do it,” she said. “I really think you could.”

Rio turned, looking her in the eyes, and she could see the honesty, the genuine belief , in those eyes. Agatha was giving her a smile that she had never seen before. It was open and bright and, just, so beautiful.

“Okay,” Rio said, turning her gaze back to the paper, “let’s start rehearsing.”

Chapter End Notes

I am sitting alone in my room, losing hope for my country. Whatever happens tomorrow, this show and your reviews have made me so happy. Thank you all. I will be fine, but I'll definitely be sad for a while.

Thankfully, writing is what makes me happy.

Chapter 3: Monologuing

Chapter Summary

Rio and Agatha do their monologues after a solid week of practicing. Will Rio make it through?

Chapter Notes

We needed a little bit of hope and fluff today.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Three

“I don’t know if I’m ready for this,” Rio said, as she and Agatha sat in the back of the auditorium, their legs resting over the back of the chairs in front of them. There were other theater kids scattered around the room, enjoying their own free periods before school ended and auditions began. Rio had spent the last week rehearsing her monologue. 

First, in Agatha’s bedroom, where she familiarized herself with the words, reading dryly the first time while Agatha gave her direction on how to change the inflection of her words, how to tilt her head or use the space around her. The piece was filled with energy, though the monologue itself was rather short. Rio’s hands shook the paper, crumpling it as she swallowed thickly, Agatha’s eyes on her making her nervous.

The first time she stuttered over a word, Agatha took her by surprise, cupping her cheeks in her hands and tilting Rio’s chin so that they were looking each other in the eyes. Her face burned where Agatha’s fingers brushed her cheeks, leaving goosebumps in their wake.

“You can do this,” Agatha said, her voice firm, almost commanding. “You have charisma. I’ve seen it, every time you hand me a flower or tell a joke that makes Alice’s drink come out her nose at lunch. You always have a snarky reply in class when some idiot boy makes a dumb, sexist joke. You can do this. Especially in front of me , your very best friend.” She gave Rio a toothy grin as she pointed to the daisy in her hair.

Rio felt herself smile back, nod. “Yeah,” she said, her voice barely more than a whisper. “Yeah, I can do this.” She stretched her neck, groaning slightly when she felt the tension seep out of it in a series of cracks. “I can do this.”

Agatha nodded and stepped back, sitting down in her chair, man-spreading as she leaned forward on her elbows. “Again,” she said.

Every time Rio missed or stumbled over a word or gave the wrong inflection, Agatha had her restart. If Rio said “um” or “uh” or took too long a pause, she made Rio restart. If her hands shook, Rio had to restart. If she wasn’t putting 110% of herself into the reading, she needed to restart.

By seven PM, they were both exhausted, but Rio still had to restart before she reached the last beat. Agatha was about to make her read again but then her eyes widened as she heard the front door open and close.

“Shit,” she said at the same time she heard her mother call her name.

“Agatha,” Evanora Harkness called up the steps, “whose filthy boots are these in my mudroom?”

“Shit, shit, shit,” Agatha said, pulling a pair of pants from her dresser and tugging them up her legs. At Rio’s look, she shrugged. “Don’t ask,” she huffed, reaching for a sweater to pull that on, as well. She took the daisy from her hair and set it in the pen cup on her desk, then turned to Rio. “Okay, you need to leave,” she said, apologetically.

“Agatha Harkness, I asked you a question,” Evanora called, the irritation evident in her voice. She heard her mother’s steps coming up the stairs and, to Rio’s surprised, dumped all the monologues and playbooks onto her bed, plucking one up and pretending to read. Rio furrowed her brow for only a second, but then the doorknob turned and she reached for a different one, pretending that she was truly as engrossed in it as Agatha.

The door opened and an older woman with graying brown hair and a severe scowl looked in on them, eyebrow raised as she eyed the two girls in the room.

“Agatha,” she said, her voice firm, “did you not hear me?”

“What?” Agatha said, feigning confusion as she looked up at her mother. “Oh, hey mom. Wait, what time is it?” She looked at her phone and let out a gasp, getting to her feet. “Rio, your mom said she wanted you home at seven , right?”

Rio immediately knew to play along. “Uh, right,” she said, shaking her head. “Why? What time is it?”

“It’s, like, 7:05.”

Rio let out an exaggerated gasp, also getting to her feet and tucking the monologue into her backpack. “I gotta go,” she said, turning to Agatha’s mother. “Thank you for your hospitality, Mrs. Harkness,” she said.

“ Miss Harkness,” Evanora corrected, somewhat coldly.

“Miss Harkness,” Rio echoed. “Thank you so much. I wish I could stay for more, uh, proper introductions, but my mom is already going to be very upset with me.” She turned to Agatha. “Thank you for the help in Drama,” she said. “I’ll see you at school.”

“I’ll walk you out,” Agatha said, practically shoving Rio toward the door, the girls brushing past her bewildered mother. “Be right back,” she said, sweetly, to Evanora.

The girls rushed downstairs, across the hall, and through the kitchen as fast as they could without absolutely sprinting .

“Thanks,” Rio said, slipping into her boots. “For the monologue. And for all the help.”

“Oh, we’re not done with that,” Agatha said. “You still haven’t gotten through that thing once without flubbing it. And you still need to practice around other people. Not just me.” She reached into the dryer next to the door, tugging out a black hoodie. 

Rio was about to reply to her when Agatha shoved the sweater over her head, tugging it down and making Rio drop her backpack to the ground behind her. Rio struggled for a few seconds before her head popped out of the hoodie, inhaling a breath.

“What the hell?” she huffed. 

“It’s dark out and actually freezing ,” Agatha said. “You can borrow this until you get yourself a coat.”

“I have a coat,” Rio huffed, but still slipped her hands into the pocket of the hoodie. It was so soft. “I just don’t usually wear it because I’m not somebody who gets cold easily.”

“Just say thank you,” Agatha huffed, rolling her eyes.

“Thank you,” Rio said, her lips quirking as she pulled a flower out of the pocket of her hoodie. It was an azalea. She offered it to Agatha, whose eyes widened, twinkled.

“Thanks,” Agatha said. Then, hearing her mother’s heavy steps as she descended the stairs, she started to shove Rio again. “Okay, you really want to get out of here,” she said. “ Trust me.”

“I’m going, I’m going,” Rio said, picking up her backpack and rushing through the garage. “See you at ninth tomorrow?” she asked, over her shoulder.

“Yes,” Agatha whispered back. “Library.”

“See you there,” Rio said, running out into the cold. Agatha had already closed the inner door.

She smelled like her the whole way home, warmed by more than just Agatha’s sweater.

 

Over the next few days, they met in the library at ninth period and relocated to the auditorium, which was usually empty (thankfully), and Rio practiced her monologue on the stage with Agatha sitting further and further back with each run-through. When more students started to pile in, however, Rio froze up, her fingers crinkling the paper.

“Hey,” Agatha would say, snapping her fingers so that Rio’s eyes were only on her, “hey, ignore them. I’m the only one here, okay?” A few students were laughing, quite audibly, behind her and she sighed, lifting her hand. Purple crackled from her fingers, and suddenly the only voice Rio could hear was Agatha’s. “I’m the only one here,” she said, soothingly. “You can’t hear them and they can’t hear you. It’s okay.” She’d learned this spell from Lilia, who used it on unruly students to shut them up so she could teach. Agatha had figured a variation of the spell that wouldn’t directly steal their voices, but just placed them in their own little bubble. Rio took a deep breath, looking down at her sheet again.

Once, they were able to sneak into the dressing room, which was much more cramped than the stage. But Rio could be louder now, could move more freely, could really act . Agatha was impressed by the way she moved, how she expressed herself, how the lines dropped off her tongue as she built her confidence. She felt a thrill down her spine when Rio looked her in the eye as she recited, really sinking into her character.

She was made breathless with each recitation, like there was some kind of spell Rio was putting on her without using any Latin or Runes, whatsoever. She really believed the words Rio was saying to her.

“That’s it,” Agatha said, getting to her feet, clapping slowly, after Rio finished her last run-through. “That’s it! You’ve done it! You fucking killed it.”

“That’s a good thing, right?” Rio asked, blushing at the look Agatha was giving her.

“Yes!” Agatha said with the same inflection one might say, “Duh!” But she was beaming at Rio, and Rio never wanted her to stop smiling like that, especially in her direction. “You did that so well! You are going to rock that audition tomorrow. Just so long as you focus on me , okay?”

Rio nodded, fighting hard not to look away from Agatha’s bright blue gaze. “Okay,” she said, clearing her throat. “Okay.”

That was just yesterday, less than twenty-four hours ago, and now they were sitting back in the auditorium, waiting for the final bell to ring. Agatha was wearing a purple suit with a crisp white shirt underneath. She had a lime green bowtie and her lipstick was redder than usual, her eyeshadow a deep purple. Her hair fell in looser curls than usual, still somewhat wild around her head, a few pieces sticking up.

“What monologue are you doing today?” Rio asked. 

Agatha grinned at her, miming a zipping motion with her fingers across her lips. This was not the first time Rio had asked the question.

“You basically picked mine for me,” Rio huffed. “You can’t even give me a hint.”

“I’m surprised you didn’t get it from my outfit, honestly,” Agatha retorted. She was wearing brown suede shoes, which she wiggled a little over the seat in front of her. Rio just shrugged. “Quite uncultured, aren’t you?”

“Is it from a movie?” Rio asked. “I don’t watch a lot of movies. I prefer books.”

“It’s kind of from books, too,” Agatha said, cryptically. “In a manner of speaking.”

“You’re giving me nothing,” Rio said, rolling her eyes. 

“I gave you that hoodie you’re wearing,” Agatha pointed out. “Which you’re going to give back when, by the way?”

When it stops smelling like you Rio wanted to say. It smelled like whatever lotion or perfume or shampoo Agatha wore that smelled like cherries and vanilla. She’d never seen Agatha wear a hoodie, but she must have used this one a lot. It had just come out of the dryer, freshly washed, but it still somehow smelled like her. Maybe it just smelled like her house.

It was comforting.

“I prefer this to a heavy winter coat,” Rio retorted. “Less to carry.”

“Especially when you’re scaling the building, right?” Agatha snorted. Rio just grinned at her.

More students had filed in as the final bells rang. Jen Kale and Alice Wu-Gulliver walked in, the former wearing a pink sweatsuit and a matching sparkly beanie on her head. She rolled her eyes as they walked past Agatha and Rio. Agatha made a face that, in turn, made Rio crack up. A freshman boy with black curly hair scurried in, as well, taking a seat across the aisle from the curls, running his hand through his hair, nervously.

Agatha gasped, leaning over to Rio, whispering in her ear, “A boy ,” she said. Not many boys at their school were interested in theater. And the ones that were usually couldn’t act or sing if their lives depended on it. Agatha wondered if this boy could do either.

Before long, the auditorium was filled with members of the drama club, both old and new. The techies all gathered in one section near the stage, a few of them looking back at Rio, who waved them off as they shot her confused glances.

The doors slammed open the next second, making both girls jump, their heads snapping in the direction of the woman who had just entered, wearing one of her colorful cardigans, carrying a mountainous pile of paper in her arms.

“I hope you’re all well-prepared,” she said, strutting down the aisle to take her seat in the third row. She placed the pile of paper down on a makeshift table that had been set up across the first two rows, then separated them into three piles. “For the uninitiated,” she said, “allow me to explain how things are going to go. This first pile,” she said, tapping a pile of blue papers on the desk, “are your sides: they are a collection of dialogues that you shall perform with one or two other students. They are not ,” she looked directly at Agatha, then Jen, “an indication of what play I have chosen for the musical. That announcement will come later, after the singing callbacks.” She moved to the next pile, printed on yellow paper. “These are your audition sheets . You will fill out your names, grades, and the titles of your audition pieces. You will then place them here, in my hands, so that I can call you up when it’s your turn. If you see me taking notes on them, do not worry. My memory is shit and I just want to remember what I thought about your audition as I watched it.” There were a few scattered laughs amongst the students. “This last pile,” she placed her hand on a pile of plain white papers, “is your contract. You sign one for every show. This says that you will show up for rehearsals when you are called, that you will not waste my time, which is precious and short as I get on in years, and that you will sell two ads in our playbill because Goddess knows we need the funding.” More laughter. “Okay, now come get your papers, in an orderly fashion . Thank you.” 

She sat down with a flourish as people began to line up. Agatha was the first one at the desk, beating Jen by a fraction of a second, and collected two of everything, handing one copy of each different-colored paper to Rio.

Rio pulled out two pens from her pocket, handing the purple one to Agatha, who smiled at her in thanks. They filled out their audition sheets in silence, then signed their contracts.

“Do I even really need to sign this?” Rio huffed. “I’m not even going to be in the show. It’s just extra credit.”

“Why not?” Agatha said, with a shrug. “Can’t hurt you.”

Rio acquiesced, writing her name in a scribble on the line. She took them back for her and Agatha, ignoring the smile and glint in Lilia’s eyes as she took them from her. 

“Nice hoodie,” Lilia said. “I didn’t know you were such a fan of Wicked .”

“Hmm?” Rio said, looking down at the word written across her shirt. “Oh, right. Yeah, I think the book was better, though.”

“Of course you do,” Lilia huffed, rolling her eyes and dismissing Rio with a wave of her hand. 

Rio went back to Agatha, who had moved up a few rows to sit in the center of the auditorium, a few rows back from Lilia, and took a seat next to her. Agatha was leaning forward now, leaning her arms on the back of the chair in front of her as her eyes roamed across the other students.

“There are five new students auditioning,” she said to Rio. “Three of them are freshmen. Two of them are boys. We are in desperate need of boys. They’ll probably get speaking roles just off that alone. If they do well in their choral audition, they’ll probably get principal roles.”

“Principal roles?” Rio asked.

“Leads,” Agatha explained. “Major speaking roles. Monologues and solos. Usually a fight scene.”

“So an Agatha role,” Rio said, smirking when Agatha turned to her with wide eyes.

“That’s what you call them?” she asked.

Rio shrugged. “You’ve played one of those prince roles-”

“Principal roles,” Agatha corrected, grinning.

“-in every single play Lilia has put on since freshman year. You are the one constant in this club. Well, you and Jen.”

“Ugh,” Agatha said, wrinkling her nose and glaring at the girl in question, who was sitting with Alice by the piano at the front of the auditorium. Alice Wu-Gulliver was the daughter of Lorna Wu, a now-retired and formerly-famous pop icon. She knew how to play pretty much every single instrument, thanks to her mother’s influence. She single-handedly ran their orchestra.

And she was Jen’s girlfriend.

Agatha would be certain that Jen was manipulating Alice if she thought Alice had anything juicy to offer her. And if she and Jen hadn’t been practically inseparable since the third grade, when Alice got detention for punching some asshole boy in the face after he attempted to dunk Jen’s face into a particularly deep puddle.

That they kind of fell in love in the meantime was happenstance.

And it was disgustingly cute. Agatha found herself staring at the two of them, sometimes, her insides twisting whenever Jen pressed a kiss to Alice’s cheek, making the shorter girl blush bright pink, or when Alice played a cute little song for Jen on the piano while they were goofing off. 

Ugh, they were gross. And gay. Very, very gay.

“Has everybody turned in their audition sheets?” Lilia called out, drawing Agatha’s attention back to her. “This is the last call. I will accept no more audition sheets after this moment. Going once…going twice…”

“Here you go!”

The freshman boy with the curly hair had sprinted up to Lilia, holding out his papers for her to take. She plucked them out of his hand with a polite smile, before mixing all the yellow audition sheets together, effectively dismissing the boy, who went to sit down next to one of the freshman techies. The other boy nudged his shoulder and got a nudge in return.

“Now,” Lilia said, “I am going to pick these names randomly. When you hear your name called, you will have ten seconds to make it to the stage before I call the next name. Capisce? We don’t have all afternoon. It’s been a long day.”

A few laughs went through the audience.

“First name,” Lilia said, plucking a sheet from the pile. “William Kaplan.”

“Billy!” the curly-haired boy said, getting to his feet. “I prefer Billy.”

“You have eight seconds, Teen,” Lilia said. “Get to the stage.”

Billy was tall and lanky, taking just a few long strikes to get to the stage. He had on a long black sweater that had seen better days and black eyeliner. He stood, nervously, in the center of the stage, blinking out past the lights.

“You may begin,” Lilia said. 

Billy cleared his throat, glancing down at the paper in his hand. “And what’s he, then, that says I play the villain when this advice is free I give and honest, probal to thinking, and indeed the course to win the Moor again?”

“Oof,” Rio whispered. “Shakespeare. Not gonna win Lilia over with that, huh?”

“He’s not doing a half-bad job, though,” Agatha whispered back. “Except for that little warble here and there. He might get a speaking role. If he can sing, he’s a shoo-in for a principal.”

Lilia interrupted Billy halfway through the monologue, thanking him for his audition. She motioned for him to leave the stage, and his shoulders slumped. He walked off, dejectedly.

“Next is…Jennifer Kale,” Lilia announced. Jen hopped up and practically skipped onto the stage while Agatha blew a raspberry.

“Real mature, Harkness,” Jen huffed as she shook out her limbs. Agatha blew another raspberry, which made Rio giggle next to her, to Agatha’s unforeseen delight. She had no idea that Rio Vidal could giggle . 

Jen’s monologue was from Aladdin - the live-action version. It was Jasmine’s monologue about..something or other. Agatha was too busy pretending to snore all through it to really pay any ounce of attention. She received a glare from Jen as she left the stage for her troubles. Agatha saluted her in response.

“Since you insist on making a spectacle of yourself, Miss Harkness,” Lilia said, “I’m sure you wouldn’t mind taking the stage next.”

“Gladly!” Agatha said, getting to her feet and tugging on the lapels of her suit jacket. She squeezed past Rio and made her way quickly to the stage, spinning with a flourish as she struck a pose in the middle of the stage, making people laugh. She took a deep breath and began: “Do I really look like a guy with a plan? You know what I am? I’m a dog chasing cars.” She slowly swept her hand from one side to the other, her gaze chasing an invisible car. Then she shook her head, rolling her eyes. “I wouldn’t know what to do with one if I caught it. You know, I just…do things. The mob has plans, the cops have plans, Gordon’s got plans.” She counted them off on her fingers. You know, they’re schemers. Schemers try to control their little worlds. I’m not a schemer.” She placed one hand on her chest. “I try to show the schemers how pathetic their attempts to control things really are. So when I say…Ah, come here.” She motioned an invisible person closer. “When I say that you and your girlfriend was nothing personal, you know that I’m telling the truth. It’s the schemers that put you where you are. You were a schemer, you had a plan, and look at where that got you.” She lifted her hands in the air, as if by example, turning in a slow circle, then pointed a finger at the audience - right at Rio. “I just did what I do best. I took your little plan and I turned it on itself. Look what I did to this city with a few drums of gas and a couple of bullets. Hmm?” She gave the audience a thoughtful expression. “You know…You know what I’ve noticed? Nobody panics when things go ‘according to plan.’ Even if the plan is horrifying ! If, tomorrow, I tell the press that, like, a gang banger will get shot, or a truckload of soldiers will be blown up, nobody panics, because it’s all part of ‘the plan’,” she curled her fingers around the words in air quotes, sneering. “But when I say that one little old mayor will die,” she pouted, mockingly, miming a tear falling down her cheek, “well then everyone loses their minds !” She rolled her eyes, clenching her jaw. “Introduce a little anarchy. Upset the established order, and everything becomes chaos.” She grinned, tilting her head to the side. “I’m an agent of chaos. Oh, and you know the thing about chaos?” She leaned toward the audience as if she were about to tell a secret. “It’s fair !”

Agatha stood straight with a smile. “Scene,” she said.

There was a round of applause and cheers as she stepped off the stage.

“Very good, Agatha,” Lilia said. “I see you’re really bringing your A-game to this audition.”

“As always,” Agatha said, confidently. Jen rolled her eyes.

“Okay,” Lilia said, leafing through her sheets. “Next is…Rio Vidal.”

There was a gasp and murmurs throughout the audience as Rio stood, awkwardly, and made her way to the front, stepping up onto the stage. Her hands were tucked into the pockets of her hoodie and she shut her eyes tight as she faced the audience. 

Rio took a deep breath, trying to remember what Agatha had coached her to do, trying to regulate her breathing, then she opened her eyes.

Agatha was in her direct line of sight, just over Lilia’s shoulder. She locked her blue eyes with Rio’s brown gaze and nodded, slightly. You can do this , Agatha mouthed, giving her a subtle thumbs up.

Rio nodded, took another deep breath.

“I wrote you that song,” she said, looking directly into Agatha’s eyes. “I wrote it because when I see you, normally, it’s just, it’s just a mess. ” She rubbed the back of her neck, looking away from Agatha, to the ground, like they’d rehearsed. Her heart was pounding in her chest. “When I think about you, I can’t breathe and I look at you,” she met Agatha’s eyes again, “and I’m not sure you’re real. You look just like…” She swallowed thickly, turning to look elsewhere in the audience, trying not to decipher the phases. “Like if someone were to say, Hey can you draw a girl and I drew you , they’d be like, hey, that’s a perfect drawing of a girl. You’re a real good artist.” She flexed her hands at her side, made them shake a little. “And my hands get all shaky when I want to touch you,” she looked at Agatha again, “and you know that great hollow feeling you get in your stomach when you see someone you’ve been thinking about for days,” she looked away from Agatha walking a little bit across stage, then back again, “and then you turn the corner,” she spun around, motioning towards Agatha, “and there they are. It’s like…” she exhaled all the air she had in her lungs until she couldn’t anymore. “And the bottom drops out and I feel like I have no actual mass or dimension and it’s like maybe I’m seeing you at that moment after having thought about you because you were, at the same time, thinking about me .” She swallowed thickly, still looking at Agatha, who didn’t look away, a soft smile on her lips. “And that’s how we ended up at the exact same place in the exact same moment.” She glanced away, suddenly very nervous. “By thinking about it that much.” She cleared her throat, glancing toward Lilia this time. “Do you need a ride?” She took a deep breath, looking down at the toes of her boots. “Scene.”

“Bravo!” Lilia said, getting to her feet. “ Superb job, Rio,” she said. “Very well done! A-plus!”

Rio beamed, her cheeks pink, as she stepped off the stage, receiving applause from the other members of the club, including her tech friends. She made her way back to Agatha’s side, hunching her shoulders as people congratulated her on a great audition. Agatha reached for her hand, squeezing it tightly as she leaned over to whisper in her ear.

“You did a great job,” she said. “You definitely earned that grade.”

“Thanks,” Rio said, still blushing, the hood of Agatha’s borrowed (stolen) hoodie partially hiding her face. “Were you…were you the Joker .”

Agatha gave her a wide, creepy grin. Rio laughed, shaking her head. 

They watched for a while more as about a dozen other students went up for their auditions, many doing a decent job, but most stumbling through their monologues. 

After the last student went, Lilia started calling up duos for the dialogues. Rio was shocked when her name was called, along with Agatha and the new kid, Billy. She walked up to Lilia with a questioning look.

“This is still part of your extra credit, doll,” she said. “Go on.”

Rio shot her a glare, but followed Agatha and Billy up, regardless, her fingers clenching around the pages as she stepped onto the stage. 

“I’ll give you a minute to prepare,” Lilia said, turning over an hourglass on her desk. 

“I’ll read for Christine,” Agatha said. “I’ve never read this play before.”

“I’ve never read any play before,” Rio huffed. “I was only supposed to do a monologue.”

“She said audition,” Agatha pointed out. “That includes more than just the monologue. It’ll be fine. Just deep breaths and it will be over before you know it.”

“I hate optimistic Agatha,” Rio grumbled. “Bring back snarky Agatha.”

“I’ll be Richard,” Billy interjected. “He’s the only guy, though I wouldn’t mind playing Amanda. She seems…neat.”

“I don’t care,” Rio groaned, shifting on her feet. “I just want to get this over with.”

“You’re Amanda,” Agatha said, meeting her eyes. “You’ll be great.”

“Yeah,” Billy said, “your audition was awesome. I loved that monologue. Where did you-”

“Times up!” Lilia said. “You may begin.”

Agatha got immediately into character, crouching over an invisible body.

“Is she dead?” Billy asked, in character, a bit nervously.

“No, she’s just napping,” Agatha replied, sarcastically. “Of fucking course she’s dead, Richard! Why would we be here if she wasn’t?”

“Is who dead?” Rio asked, a bit stiffly, pretending to try to look over Agatha’s shoulder. Agatha ignored her as she stood, removing fake gloves.

“Based on temp, I’d say she died within the last hour,” Agatha said, shaking her head. “Shame. She was young. Pretty.”

“Who was pretty?” Rio said, loosening up a little. Agatha continued to block her view. “Hey, I have that sweater.” She looked down at herself. “I’m wearing that sweater. Who did you say that was?”

“Is there any ID on the body?” Billy asked, trying to make his voice deeper. It didn’t work very well as his voice broke, making some people in the audience snicker. 

“None that I saw,” Agatha said. “Her name tag says ‘Amanda’ though, you see that?” She pointed down at the invisible body.

“ My name is Amanda,” Rio said, slowly. “Wait-”

“What did she die of?” Billy and Rio asked in sync.

“Blunt force trauma to the head,” Agatha said. Rio rubbed the back of her head, slowly, wincing as if something stung her. She looked at her hand, imagining blood there. It made her jump. “It came from behind. Poor thing never saw it coming.” She crossed her arms over her chest.

“I wonder who could have done this,” Billy said. “Who would have wanted her dead?”

“Could be anybody, as far as we know,” Agatha said. “Jilted lover, estranged sibling, hell, even a psychotic stranger. We won’t know more until we get her back to the lab.”

“This can’t be how it ends,” Rio said, stepping in front of Agatha. She was about an inch taller than the other girl, but she met Rio’s eyes, the flicker of a smile on her lips before she readjusted, looking past Rio as if not seeing her. “I had so much I wanted to accomplish. I’m still young. Can’t you do anything?”

“Do a tox screen,” Agatha said, ignoring her. “Tell them to put a rush on it.”

“But she died of blunt force trauma, didn’t she?” Billy pointed out.

“Doesn’t mean she wasn’t on something. What else would she be doing in this part of town?”

“I don’t do drugs,” Rio insisted, grabbing Agatha’s shoulders. “I don’t even drink ! Not really. I have a job - a good one! I don’t…” she looked around, “I don’t know what’s happening. I don’t belong here. This can’t be the end!”

“Let me know what the results show,” Agatha said, walking away.

“Will do,” Billy replied, looking down at the invisible body. “We’ll figure it out,” he promised. “We always do.” He walked in the opposite direction, leaving Rio to look down at the invisible body alone.

“Why me?” she said, her voice cracking.

When the applause started behind her, she jumped a little, turning to see Lilia beaming at her, back on her feet. Her entire body went hot as she jumped back off the stage and walked up the aisle, but she didn’t stop. She kept going until she was outside, the chill January air slapping her face. She shivered.

“Rio?” 

Rio spun around to see Agatha there, in only her suit and her purple beanie on her head. She was also shivering, but she looked more concerned as she stepped toward Rio. 

“Are you okay?” Agatha asked. She had never seen Rio like this. The girl was all sly grins and quiet stares and random flowers that grew in her hands. 

“I’m fine,” Rio lied, looking down. “I just…that was a lot. A lot of people looking at me, judging me.”

Agatha furrowed her brow. “Nobody was judging you,” she said. “Quite the opposite, actually. They were applauding you, celebrating you. You put on a wonderful performance.” 

“I hate having the spotlight on me,” Rio huffed, a puff of smoke appearing before her lips. 

“It’s just for today,” Agatha said, shrugging one shoulder. “Just for you to get your A, right? And now it’s over.”

Rio smiled as the truth of those words hit her. By Friday - just two days away - Lilia will have announced and casted the whole show, and she could start working with tech crew to build the new set.

“Right,” she said, breathing a long sigh of relief. “Yeah. Oh, thank God that’s over.” She bent over at the waist for a second, swallowing thickly. “Thank you,” she said to Agatha, “for everything.”

“Hey,” Agatha replied, “what are best friends for?”

“You’re keeping up the best friends thing?” Rio laughed. “Really?”

Agatha pouted. “You don’t wanna be my best friend anymore?” She mimed a tear coming down her cheek and Rio laughed, rolling her eyes. 

“Does being your best friend mean I get to keep this sweater a little bit longer?”

“Are you going to keep giving me flowers?” Agatha asked, her cheeks going a bit pink.

Rio smiled and held out her hand, producing a snowdrop. She offered it to Agatha, who took it with a smile.

“You can have it for a little bit longer,” Agatha said. “But I’m going to eventually want it back.”

“Sure, bestie,” Rio said, stepping closer and slinging her arm around Agatha’s shoulders. “Now let’s get you inside before you turn to ice.” Agatha shivered under her arm as they headed back into the auditorium.

“Good plan,” she said, tucking the snowdrop into her jacket pocket.

When they got back inside, Lilia was standing on the stage with a paper in her hands. “Quiet down, everyone,” she said as Rio and Agatha took their seats in the fifth row, the latter covering herself with her coat to warm up. “Okay, so now I’m going to need the following students back here tomorrow for the choral audition. If your name is not called, that doesn’t mean that you didn’t get a part; it just means that I need to see some specific people for what I have in mind. Listen for your name, please.” She cleared her throat. “Jennifer Kale. Agatha Harkness. Naturally.” She rolled her eyes. “Billy Kaplan. Rio Vidal. Theo…”

Rio’s ears started ringing after she heard her name. She and Agatha shared a look, staring at one another in shock. This wasn’t supposed to be happening. She wasn’t supposed to have a choral audition. Lilia had only told her to do a monologue. She’d gotten roped into doing the little scene, but that was really just supposed to be all. She couldn’t sing . Not in front of others .

When Lilia finished reading off the list, she addressed the crowd again. “Your choral audition is at 2:30 pm sharp ,” she said. “This one will be a little more private, in the chorus room. It will be accompanied by our own Alice Wu-Gulliver.” She motioned toward Alice, who was still sitting at the piano. There was a smattering of applause. “Alice will be choosing a generic showtune for everyone to sing, both as a group and individually. Make sure you drink something nice and warm to loosen up those vocal chords. Have a nice evening and I shall see you all tomorrow!”

And, with a flourish, she started toward the door.

Rio watched her go, anger growing in her chest. There was no way she was going to sing in front of anybody .
