“No, you won’t,” Rio huffed. “Because then I’ll drop out of the play.”

“What?” Agatha gasped, frowning at her. “You wouldn’t.”

Rio immediately softened at that. “No,” she said, taking Agatha’s hand. “No, I wouldn’t.” She glared at Lilia, who just smirked triumphantly back at her. “No funny business,” she said. “That doesn’t include kissing , does it? Or hickeys?”

“Clothes stay on,” Lilia huffed. “And hands stay above the belt.”

“We don’t wear belts,” Agatha pointed out.

“I’m about to rescind the offer,” Lilia said, humorlessly.

“Fine, fine,” Rio said, holding up her hands in surrender. “PG-rated date, we get it.”

“Maybe PG-13,” Agatha murmured. 

Lilia rolled her eyes and pretended she didn’t hear that. “You can come during ninth period,” she said. “I’ll make myself scarce. You’ll have until four PM to canoodle and whatnot.”

“ Canoodle ?” Agatha snorted. Her laugh died on her lips at the look Lilia shot her. “Okay,” she said. “Thank you, Lilia.”

“Damn right, thank you Lilia,” Lilia huffed. “Make sure you clean up all the flowers and food wrappers and whatnot. I don’t want to find a single petal or piece of trash anywhere in this room, capisce?” The girls nodded. “Good. Now leave. Let me finish my lunch in peace.”

“Um, actually,” Agatha said. “We were wondering if we could eat in here with you…”

“Seriously,” Lilia all but whined. “Why?”

Agatha shrugged. “It’s the only place Rio and I can really talk and hold hands and stuff.”

“And get away from the smell of the dressing room,” Rio added, wrinkling her nose. “I’ll give you a pastel .” She pulled a bag of them from her backpack. “My mom made them.”

Lilia pressed her lips together for a moment before snatching the bag. “Gimme that,” she said, opening it. She pulled out one of the pasteles, which was surprisingly warm. “Is this thing enchanted?” she asked, handing the bag back to Rio.

“The pasteles are,” Rio said, handing one to Agatha. “The bag is just a bag.”

Rio and Agatha each took a seat at a desk in the front row of the class while Lilia took her seat at her own desk, pushing aside her salad in favor of the pastel .

 

The next day, Agatha wore a white sweater with black hearts all over it, the hem and sleeves a little long on her, the former covering most of her black skirt, which went down to her knees. She also had on black heart earrings. She had snuck out of the house while her mother was still drinking her coffee, intent on not having to explain why she was dressed so festively when, for all her mother knew, she was still single.

If her mother even had an inkling that Agatha was dating somebody, she would demand to meet them. Agatha didn’t hate any boy enough to drag them into that. She also didn’t like any boy enough to trust them with that privileged look into her life.

Except maybe Billy.

But he was practically a toddler, so that was gross.

Her mother hadn’t called her to come back in and say a ‘proper goodbye’ as she’d done a few times in the past, which was a good sign. Agatha made it all the way to the school without incident. Barely anybody was there as she’d gotten there nearly 20 minutes earlier than usual, which meant that she had time to just relax at a table in the library, checking over the homework she had done the night before.

Her phone chimed in her pocket and Agatha pulled it out, smiling at the text from ‘Ralph’ (Rio’s code name in her phone) asking her if she was free for tutoring after school. Her mother had finally given back her phone, and Rio had downloaded one of those apps that gave her a fake number so that Evanora wouldn’t catch on. 

As far as Evanora knew, Ralph was one of the kids on the basketball team that Agatha occasionally tutored so he didn’t get kicked off the team. There was even a kid named Ralph on the team, and he was dumb enough that Harper would probably believe Agatha was his tutor. But they didn’t talk. And Agatha had placed a hex on his feeble mind so that he would confirm anytime anybody asked him if he being tutored by Agatha.

It was harmless. Mostly.

Mind spells could be tricky.

He’d probably be fine.

“Ninth period,” Agatha texted ‘Ralph’, smirking when she got a thumbs up in response.

She reached into her backpack, where she felt for Rio’s gift, which she’d wrapped in newspaper (they didn’t have any wrapping paper at home and she didn’t want to raise suspicion) and smiled softly. 

Rio had been teasing her for the last week about what she’d give her for Valentine’s Day, even before they had decided to do their picnic. She had promised thousands of flowers. Agatha had reminded her that that many flowers would certainly suffocate them in such a small dressing room. Rio had said that she’d cover the field outside the school with them then, making a rainbow of flowers that would spread across Westview and not die until the following winter.

Agatha had rolled her eyes and called her cheesy, but she’d laid her head on Rio’s shoulder, anyway, listening to her list every single flower she planned to make grow for Agatha, to show her how she felt about her when she couldn’t use her words.

Agatha had wanted to say “I love you” at that very moment, but she bit back the words. It was too soon. They hadn’t even officially been dating for a month yet. This thing between them was still so new even though it felt as though they’d been doing this for years – centuries even.

And while she was pretty sure that Rio would return the sentiment, she couldn’t be entirely sure, and that was enough to give her pause. She’d say it one day, she was certain, but for now the words could wait. Maybe until she was free to say it aloud in front of other people without worrying what it could mean for her future.

During lunch, Agatha sat with Billy, who showed her the texts that Eddie had been sending him all day, full of hearts and declarations of affection. He had a sprig of baby’s breath that he twirled between his fingers, blushing whenever Agatha teased him about it. 

“It symbolizes new beginnings,” Billy told her. “Eddie gave it to me when he asked if I’d be his boyfriend. Now he just gives me a sprig anytime we’re together.”

“Aww,” Agatha said, a little bit sarcastically. She wasn’t jealous. Not at all. 

Shut up.

She glanced sideways at where Rio was sitting with Jen and Alice. The two other girls also had flowers. Jen had a full bouquet of carnations in her hands, and Alice had one of them tucked behind her ear, laughing at something that Jen had just said. Rio locked eyes with Agatha for a second, shooting her a grin before she glanced down at the bag of chips that constituted her lunch.

Agatha sighed and looked back at Billy, who gave her a sympathetic look.

“Soon,” he mouthed, going back to the tuna sandwich he’d been eating. Agatha just nodded.

She glanced around the room, her eyes finding Harper Caulfield, who was still dealing with the issue of her nose growing and shrinking with every lie and truth she told, respectively. She was a natural gossip, even when she knew the information she was spreading was not rooted in fact, so this was a difficult time for her, to say the least.

She was staring down into a container of what looked like potato salad, frowning as her nose practically touched the table. Agatha smirked, turning back to her own lunch and taking a satisfied bite.

 

By the time ninth period rolled around, Agatha was practically bouncing on her heels in excitement, making sure that nobody was following her as she approached Lilia’s classroom.

Lilia answered the door when she knocked, welcoming her in as she talked loudly about the essay that Agatha had just handed in the afternoon prior. Agatha rolled her eyes at the over-the-top acting. How this woman had ever appeared on stage was beyond her.

Lilia shut the door and placed a soundproofing enchantment on it, then placed a glamour on the door’s window and the exterior windows to make it look as though she and Agatha were just discussing her paper, then she made her way to the door that led into the balcony of the theater, giving Agatha one more warning glance as she disappeared.

Rio came through the window five minutes later, wearing a dress shirt, blazer, and a green tie that she hadn’t had on earlier. Her hair was in a bun, too, and she had a rose between her teeth as she hopped down from the counter and bowed in front of Agatha.

“You are so extra,” Agatha huffed, but her heart swelled with affection as she took the rose that Rio offered her, pulling the other girl into a kiss that made them both weak in the knees. 

“You haven’t seen anything yet,” Rio said. “Look outside.”

“You didn’t…” Agatha gasped, running to the window. Her eyes widened at the sight of a large heart in the snow, the green grass invisible beneath an array of flowers, mostly violets and lilacs and lilies – all Agatha’s favorites.

“It’s not exactly what I promised,” Rio said, rubbing the back of her neck bashfully, “but I think I did a pretty good job.”

“It’s incredible,” Agatha sighed, turning to cup Rio’s cheeks in her hands. She pressed a soft kiss on her lips before reaching for her backpack and pulling out the newspaper-wrapped gift. “It’s not much,” she said, “but it was made with love…and no small amount of my hair.”

Rio furrowed her brow at that as she tore through the newspaper, pulling out a forest green infinity scarf, hand-crocheted, made of impossibly soft yarn. She immediately wrapped it thrice around her neck, smiling happily. It smelled like Agatha, like cedarwood and lavender and her coconut-scented shampoo. Rio made a happy noise in the back of her throat that would have been embarrassing if she wasn’t so filled with joy.

“So you like it?” Agatha laughed, grinning at her.

“I love it,” Rio said, stepping forward, the scarf still wrapped around her. “Thank you so much. This must have taken forever to make.”

Agatha shrugged. “Crochet goes by pretty quickly, actually,” she said. “Especially when you use magic to help.”

“Isn’t that cheating?” Rio asked.

“Isn’t using magic to grow flowers cheating ?” Agatha retorted, raising one eyebrow.

“Touche,” Rio snorted, pulling down the scarf so she could press a kiss to Agatha’s lips. “Happy Valentine’s Day, Agatha,” she whispered against her lips.

“Happy Valentine’s Day, Rio,” Agatha whispered back. Those stupid words were on her tongue again, so she deepened the kiss, hoping that Rio could feel them with every swipe of her lips and tongue. 

When they pulled back, both girls were grinning, their foreheads pressed together. Rio’s eyes were so soft and her mouth opened and closed a few times, as though she were debating on saying something. Agatha’s heart seized in her chest, hope warming her for a fleeting moment.

“You hungry?” Rio asked, finally, clearing her throat when it cracked. “My mom packed us some food.”

“Is it as good as what you brought yesterday?” Agatha asked.

“Better,” Rio promised. “Mofongo, rice and beans, and pasteles, leftover from yesterday.” She pulled away and dug through her bag, which must have been just as enchanted as the food because there was no way she had enough room for all of that plus her books. She had two big containers of food, all mixed together. She handed one to Agatha, then she reached back into the bag and pulled out two glass Coke bottles and some utensils. 

“Did you get that bag from Mary Poppins?” Agatha teased, setting herself up at one of the desks with her food. 

“It’s a simple enough enchantment,” Rio said. “I’ll show you how to do it, if you want.” She stood up with her own food and pushed a desk to touch Agatha’s so that they were sitting together. She handed her one of the Cokes and used a bottle opener to take off the cap, clinking her own with it. “ Salud .”

“Health?” Agatha asked, smirking at her.

“It’s closer to ‘cheers’ in this context,” Rio informed her. “It’s just something we say.”

“I like it,” Agatha said. “ Salud .” She clinked their bottles again and took a sip. Rio mirrored her, smiling around the rim of her bottle. “So,” Agatha said, “what are we watching with our meal?”

“Something romantic?” Rio asked. “ Imagine Me & You ?”

“You don’t know who Roger Rabbit is, but you know that movie?” Agatha teased.

“It has lesbians in it,” Rio huffed. “Of course I know it.” Agatha lifted her brows. “My mom went through this whole phase of making me watch exclusively gay movies after I came out. We had to stop halfway through Bound .”

Agatha’s eyes widened at that and she pressed her hand to her mouth, suppressing her laugh. “Oh no,” she said.

“Oh yes ,” Rio groaned. “Very awkward moment. My dad was there, too.”

“The fact that you didn’t die of embarrassment is a testament to your strength,” Agatha teased. “Still, it feels a little on the nose, don’t you think? How about Bodies, Bodies, Bodies ?”

“Isn’t that a horror movie?” Rio asked. “I saw the trailer,” she explained at Agatha’s look of surprise.

“It is a horror movie,” Agatha confirmed, “but it also has lesbians in it. And drama. We could also watch Bottoms .”

“That sounds sexual,” Rio said. “I figured we should steer clear of sexual.”

“It’s really not,” Agatha said. “It’s funny as hell is what it is.”

“Whatever you want, babe,” Rio said, taking a sip of her Coke. “You’re the expert on all the modern media, after all.”

“Let’s flip a coin,” Agatha said. “Tails for Bottoms , Heads for Bodies .”

“Do you make all your decisions this way?” Rio asked, grinning as Agatha pulled a coin out of her pocket. 

“Not all of them,” Agatha said, leaning over to kiss her. Rio hummed against her lips. Agatha pulled away with a grin, then flipped the coin. “ Bottoms it is,” she said, pulling out her phone and pulling up the movie on Amazon. “You’re going to love this, I promise.”

Rio was smiling softly at her, something sparkling in her eyes that Agatha couldn’t – or was too scared to – name. She ignored it, placing the phone against a book in front of them so that it stayed standing, and pressed play on the movie. Then she dug into the food, letting out a moan at the flavor that met her tongue.

“This is so good,” she said. “Is your mom single, by any chance?”

“Oh my Goddess,” Rio huffed. “Shut up.” She nudged Agatha’s shoulder, shaking her head.

“I mean,” Agatha said, “if you can cook like this, too, that would be great.”

“I made the rice,” Rio informed her. 

Agatha took a bite of the rice and let out another moan. “Fantastic,” she said, pressing a kiss to Rio’s cheek. “No notes.”

Rio snorted and kissed her temple as they settled in to watch their movie.

Chapter End Notes

Will they ever stop being cowards and say those three little words to each other?

Yes, Harper is based off of the character from Happiest Season, who did not deserve Abby OR Riley (they clearly deserved each other) and I will forever be salty about that ending.

Anyway, comments would make an awesome late Christmas gift.

Chapter 13: Fuck

Chapter Summary

Agatha has a little stress in her life and starts to realize just how many people want to support her...and that scares the living shit out of her.

Chapter Notes

I can't even begin to express how devastated I am by this morning's news. Reminder to everybody to give Aubrey the time and space to grieve for the foreseeable future. And do not judge the way she grieves if she happens to jump right back into work. All my love and support to her and their family. Rest in peace, Jeff Baena.

Sorry if this is how you're finding out, by the way.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Chapter Thirteen

When Agatha got home after rehearsal that day, she was exhausted , but there was also a happy flutter in her stomach as she twirled the violet Rio had given her at the end of their date. She knew she couldn’t have brought home a whole bouquet without arousing suspicion from her mother, but a single violet couldn’t hurt. Agatha had been bringing flowers home for a good three years now and her mother hadn’t noticed even once . Why would she notice now?

The house was silent when Agatha stepped inside. She tried to ignore the chill that went down her spine at this as she toed off her boots and unwound her scarf from her neck, hanging it up along with her coat. She tucked the violet into her backpack, making sure that it wouldn’t accidentally get crushed on her way to her bedroom, and ascended the steps. 

Her mother was usually home by now. Her car had been in the driveway. It was possible she might have been picked up by a friend or walked somewhere close by, but both were unlikely. Evanora rarely left the house on a weeknight after work. Unless there was a special occasion that Agatha was unaware of.

They never celebrated Valentine’s Day. Evanora had no beaus, as far as Agatha knew, and any holiday that they did celebrate, Agatha figured she would know about, wouldn’t she? Her mother didn’t celebrate quietly; everything was a performance for her.

“Mother?” she called out as she reached the top of the stairs. “Are you home?”

Silence.

That unnerved Agatha even more.

She walked slowly down the hall, pausing at her mother’s door, which, as always, was firmly shut. Agatha knocked three times. The door was unlocked, as she knew, but if she dared to open it before knocking, her mother’s spell would alert her. 

There was no answer. Agatha knocked again, three raps on the door.

Still no answer.

She shrugged and moved on, looking at her phone to confirm the time. It was after seven already. This was very unusual. Agatha frowned, checking to see if she had missed any calls or texts from her mother telling her that she would be home late or that she had missed some important dinner for one of their church friends.

She found nothing.

“Mother?” she called again. She stopped at the bathroom door and knocked. Evanora had her own bathroom attached to her room, but it wasn’t too out of the ordinary for her to use Agatha’s.

It was empty.

Agatha let out a soft hum and continued to the end of the hall, where her room sat. The door was ajar.

Her eyes widened at that. She had never left her door ajar when she was leaving the house. She had a similar spell on her room that her mother had had. If anybody knocked on it, she would know. If anybody entered it, she would know immediately. If the door knob was turned enough to open even the tiniest bit, she should have been alerted. 

Why was she not alerted?

Agatha rushed into her room, shoving the door open. Her eyes went wide as she saw the disarray of her usually very neat space. Everything was knocked off her desk; her bedsheets and blankets were pulled off the bed along with her pillows, scattered on the floor; her posters were torn down, some even ripped and hanging off of the wall, every drawer in the room opened, along with her closet, clothes strewn all over the floor; and one of her lamps was broken in two.

“Mom?” Agatha called out, suddenly panicking as tears filled her eyes. She turned back toward the hallway, pulling out her phone again. “Mom? Are you home?”

There was still no answer. 

Agatha opened her mother’s contact and pressed the ‘Call’ button, tears falling down her cheeks as she crept back down the stairs, one of her hands glowing purple while the other pressed the phone to her ear. 

“Pick up,” she murmured, her heart hammering in her chest. “Pick up, pick up.”

Then she heard it. The ringing.

It was coming from her mother’s office on the first floor. Agatha took the phone away from her ear, but she didn’t yet hang up. Instead, she followed the ringing down the hall to where her mother’s office door was cracked open, very much like her own had been. Agatha crept toward it, peeking inside.

Evanora Harkness was tapping furiously at her keyboard, her brow furrowed over the top edge of her glasses. Her mouth was turned down in an almost cartoon-y frown. She looked…displeased. Her phone sat silently next to her, even as it continued to ring in Agatha’s ear. 

She must have had it on silent. Or she was blocking her daughter’s calls. Either seemed just as likely to Agatha, who rolled her eyes and hung up just as Evanora’s voicemail answered.

“Mom?” Agatha said, knocking lightly at the door. Evanora looked up, her brows still furrowed as she glared up at her daughter. She didn’t speak. “I called out for you when I got home,” Agatha said, cautiously pushing the door open a bit more. “You didn’t answer.”

“I’m busy, Agatha,” Evanora huffed. She looked back at her computer, frown still firmly in place on her laptop.

“I figured,” Agatha said, trying not to roll her eyes and just barely succeeding. “What…what happened to my room? It looks like somebody broke in.”

“Oh,” Evanora said, not even bothering to pay a glance to her. “I was looking for something.”

“Okay…” Agatha said. “Could I ask what that something is? I had all my stuff neatly organized. I could probably have told you where it is.”

“Don’t give me attitude, Agatha Harkness,” Evanora huffed, turning the full force of her glare back on the teen.

“I’m not,” Agatha said, trying to keep her voice even and calm, like she was talking to a wild animal (though it wasn’t too far off from the truth). “I just…I’m a little upset that you didn’t just ask me if you needed something. I could have helped you. That’s all.”

“I was perfectly capable of searching my house on my own, Agatha.”

“Right,” Agatha sighed, shutting her eyes tightly for a moment, taking a deep breath through her nose. “Did you find what you were looking for?” she asked, opening them back up.

“No.” She did not expand.

“Well, what is it that you were looking for?” Agatha asked, starting to lose her patience.

“Not important anymore,” Evanora said. “Now, leave me be. I have work I still need to finish.”

Agatha sighed. “Okay,” she said. “Um, did you eat dinner already?”

“I made toast,” Evanora said. “I’m not very hungry. Leave.”

“Okay,” Agatha sighed. “I’m leaving. Do you want the door closed?”

“Was it closed when you entered?” 

“...no.”

“Then I think you have your answer.”

Agatha took another breath, pulling the door almost closed as she left her mother’s office and headed down the hall to make herself something quick to eat for dinner. Her backpack was still on her back.

 

“I spent two hours putting my room back together,” Agatha told Billy the next day at lunch. “And that was before I had to start on my homework for APES.” She rolled her eyes, taking another bite of her sandwich. “If I run away from home, could I still live with your family?” she asked. “Or would that put your brother’s adoption in jeopardy?”

“Well,” Billy said, “you’re seventeen. Almost eighteen. And Tommy’s been legally adopted for, like, two years now. I think we’re good on that front. If my parents were still fostering him, that would be a different story. Not sure how they’d handle a runaway, though.”

“Maybe I can just live in the dressing room,” Agatha said. “I can make a blanket and some pillows out of the old costumes in there. I’ll be nice and warm cuddled up with some old Renaissance gowns, don’t you think?”

Billy snorted. “I’m sure you’ll find somewhere to go,” he said. “Maybe you can stay with Miss Lilia.”

“It’s just Lilia, kid,” Agatha snorted. “If you call her ‘Miss’ to her face, she’ll put a hex on you, I’m sure of it.”

“Thanks for the warning. Why do we call her by her first name?”

Agatha shrugged. “She hates the formality of Miss Calderu . She said they called her that for decades before she got tenure. Now, she does whatever the hell she wants.” Agatha snorted, shaking her head. “She’s the best, isn’t she?”

“Then you should live with her,” Billy said. “I’m sure she’d welcome you. I’m, like, ninety percent certain that you’re already her favorite student?”

“Only ninety percent?” Agatha asked, raising one brow. “What’s the other ten percent?”

“You annoy the heck out of her,” Billy said.

Agatha rolled her eyes. “ Heck ?” she huffed. “Billy, you’ve been a man for what, like eighteen months now?”

“Twenty-two,” he corrected, blushing. 

“Whatever. You gotta start using your man words. It’s hell , not heck.”

“Whatever,” Billy said.

“Not whatever . If you’re going to be a bad-ass witch someday, you need to actually be able to say the word bad-ass. Come on! Say it!”

“I don’t want to,” Billy muttered, looking down at his lunch.

“Say it,” Agatha said, pointing at him in the face. “Say it, say it, say it!” She started pounding her fists on the table. 

“Oh my God,” Billy said, his face turning bright red as everybody else at the table turned to stare at them. He shrunk down in his seat. “Stop.”

“Say it!” Agatha continued to chant. “Say it, say it, say it!”

Others around them started to chant as well, surprising Billy, whose eyes became as big as saucers. “Say it, say it, say it!” they chanted, banging their own fists on the table.

“What are we trying to get the newb to say?” Olivia whispered in Agatha’s ear. She obviously hadn’t been listening to their conversation despite being immediately to Agatha’s right.

“ Bad-ass ,” Agatha said. “Or any curse, really. This schoolboy needs to toughen up!” She grinned evilly back at Billy, joining the chant again. “Say it! Say it, say it, say it!”

“Fine!” Billy exclaimed, looking around the table at the other members of their club. He rolled his eyes, took a deep breath and said, “Bad-ass.”

“That’s it ?” Sonny exclaimed from the other end of the table. “Surely, you can do better than that, Kaplan. Come on! Say the f word!”

“Why don’t you say the f word?!” Billy shot back.

“Because my grandma might hear me,” Sonny said, “and I wouldn’t want to break her fragile little heart.” They put their hand over their heart, solemnly. “Your granny ain’t here though, is she? Say the f word.”

Billy blushed deeply, looking all around. Some kids from other tables were now looking at him, expectantly. His blush deepened and he covered his face, letting out a groan.

“You’re all vultures,” he huffed. “Every one of you.” He uncovered his face and rolled his eyes. “Fine,” he said, clearing his throat. “Fuck,” he murmured, so low that only the people immediately surrounding him could recognize the curse for what it was.

“Seriously?” Agatha cackled. “Come on, pet ,” she said. “You can do better than that! Louder! Louder, louder, louder!”

This started a whole new chant. Now people from other tables were joining in. Billy blushed from the tips of his ears down underneath the neckline of his sweater. If he looked at his hands, he was certain they’d be flushed as well.

“Fuck!” he yelled, a little too loudly.

“ Mister Kaplan! ” a voice scolded from the doorway of the cafeteria. Billy blanched when he saw Principal Davis standing there, hands on her hips as she glared at him. “My office,” she ordered. “ Now. ”

“But, I–” he stammered, looking between her, Agatha, and the rest of their table.

“ Now .” Principal Davis’s voice left no room for argument. 

Billy sent Agatha a terrified look as he stood up from the table, gathering his belongings, and made to follow the principal out of the cafeteria. Agatha stood up, too, wrapping up the rest of her lunch and following him toward the principal. He furrowed his brow at her in confusion. Agatha just sent him a soft, supportive smile.

“Miss Harkness,” Principal Davis said, “what are you doing? I didn’t ask for you to follow us.”

“But I should,” Agatha said. “This is all my fault.”

“How so?” the principal asked, suspiciously.

“I told him to do it,” Agatha said, shrugging one shoulder. 

“Still,” the principal said, “that doesn’t mean he had to–”

“I led the whole cafeteria in a chant,” Agatha said. “We pressured him into doing it. It’s mostly my fault, though. Two minutes ago, Billy wouldn’t say anything worse than ‘heck’. He’s a pussy.”

“Hey!” Billy whined. “I am not .”

“You whine like a pussy,” Agatha retorted, lifting one eyebrow in mirth.

“Agatha!” Principal Davis exclaimed. “Language!” 

“Sorry,” Agatha said. “But, really, it’s my fault. He shouldn’t get in trouble for this. He’s just a good little jewish boy.” She patted Billy’s hand, and he knocked her hand away, frowning at her. Agatha grinned at him, before turning a serious face to Principal Davis. “If anybody should be punished, it should be me.”

Principal Davis considered her for a moment, frowning between Agatha and Billy before sighing and rolling her eyes. “Fine,” she said. “Mr. Kaplan, go back to your lunch. Miss Harkness, follow me.”

Agatha nodded, ignoring Billy as he tried to protest and shoving him back in the direction of their table, giving him a stern look as she made to follow the principal. Billy watched her go for a long moment before he finally turned and headed back to his seat, blushing as his tablemates clapped for him.

 

“You’re a straight-A student,” Principal Davis said, reading from a file, “but that doesn’t preclude you from getting into trouble.”

“Yeah,” Agatha sighed, relaxing back into her seat across from Principal Davis, “I know. You’re not gonna call my mother, are you?” That was the thing she was most afraid of. So long as she did well in school and didn’t get into any trouble and nobody called Evanora, she was fine. This put that all in jeopardy.

She should have let that little twink take the fall.

“I have no interest in speaking with your mother,” Principal Davis retorted, wrinkling her nose. Agatha let out a sigh of relief. “But I am interested in why one of my best students would want to corrupt one of the younger ones. You’ve never me trouble before today; what’s going on?”

“Nothing,” Agatha replied, maybe too quickly. “Nothing. I just…it was just supposed to be a joke, you know? I was just teasing him. It got out of hand is all.”

“Mmhmm,” Principal Davis said, eyeing her suspiciously. “And how are things going at home? You getting enough attention there?”

Agatha scoffed before she could stop herself. At the look Principal Davis sent her, she cleared her throat. “I’m getting as much attention as I’ve always gotten,” she said. “I swear.”

“Which, I’m guessing,” the principal said, “isn’t much. Is it?”

Agatha looked away. It wasn’t a very big secret with most of her teachers over the years that her mother wasn’t especially attentive . Evanora rarely attended parent-teacher conferences. When she did, she never had anything to say about Agatha’s work or the exemplary work she did. Even when Agatha’s teachers gushed over her daughter, Evanora had not even pretended to care how well she was doing, how celebrated she was by her teachers, even the ones that she annoyed. She had a perfect GPA and still Evanora never gave any sort of indication that Agatha was doing a good job. 

Agatha’s cheeks burned with shame. She felt a lump form in her throat as she looked down at her lap, tears gathering at the corners of her eyes. She felt a hand on her shoulder and jumped, looking up at Principal Davis, whose own eyes looked wet.

The principal was a kind older woman. Her hair was gray and shoulder-length, straight as a pin. Her face was lined and soft, her blue eyes filled with empathy. She was a witch, too, Agatha knew, but she was much younger than Lilia despite her appearances. She was one of the witches who preferred to age like the majority of humans, looking every bit like any average sixty-something year old granny. Sonny looked a lot like her, Agatha noticed, with the same blue eyes and kind smile. Agatha wondered how supportive Principal Davis was of them and their gender identity.

“I can help, you know,” the principal said, “in whichever way you feel most comfortable. I can set you up with the school guidance counselor or…something else? Anything, sweetheart. You just name it.”

She crouched down next to Agatha’s chair, and Agatha could tell that the move made her uncomfortable. Principal Davis winced a little bit when her knees let out an audible creak, but she didn’t complain one bit. She stayed in the position, giving Agatha all of her attention, searching Agatha’s eyes. Agatha bit her lip, shaking her head, and Principal Davis seemed to understand. She nodded, pushing herself back up into a standing position, grimacing as her knees cracked.

“You know,” she said, “Sonny is my grandchild.” She motioned to the photo of a young child, maybe age seven, smiling up at the camera, a Mickey Mouse hat on their head, Principal Davis’s arms wrapped around them. The child wore a Buzz Lightyear shirt and a pair of denim shorts. They were missing three teeth. Their hair was in pigtails over their shoulders. There was a bandage on their chin and skin peeling from a sunburned nose. “We should have known a long time ago,” she said, “that they weren’t what we thought they were. They were always on the masculine side, always preferring boys’ clothes. Back in my day, we just called that a tomboy.” She shrugged, smiling affectionately as she picked up the photo, running her thumb over the glass. “When they were ten, they told their parents that they didn’t feel like a girl. So their parents brought them to a therapist.” She frowned, glaring out into space. “Awful man. Do you know what he recommended?” Agatha shook her head. “ Corrective gender therapy. That’s what he called it, anyway. He said that Sonny was just confused. That they needed to be taught how to be a girl because obviously their mother wasn’t doing a good enough job.” She rolled her eyes. “Mind you, I have three granddaughters.” She motioned to the other photographs on her desk and the counter behind her desk. “Two of them are older than Sonny; both of them are very feminine. They always did makeup, played dress-up, played with dolls…you name it. Their younger sister is like that, too. Sonny was just…Sonny.” She smiled affectionately at the picture again. “My daughter told that doctor where he could shove it,” she said, placing the picture down. “They found another therapist who specialized in gender- affirming therapy. She explained that what Sonny was experiencing wasn’t confusion about their gender, but rather frustration with not understanding why others weren’t perceiving them correctly. That’s what the doctor said, anyway. I just knew that my baby was in pain and we wanted to find a way to stop it.”

She sat back down, looking at Agatha from across the desk as she folded her hands together. “It took a long time for Sonny to finally come to terms with their identity, but we, as their family, were there with them every step of the way.” Agatha nodded, feeling a tear fall down her cheek as her chest ached. “Agatha, sweetie,” Principal Davis asked, leaning forward, “do you have that?”

Agatha’s eyes widened and her breathing went a little shallow as she stood up, immediately reaching for her bag. “I’m fine,” she said, inching toward the door. “Really. I just…I’m sorry about Billy.” She shrugged her shoulders. “It won’t happen again.”

“Agatha,” Principal Davis started, her voice cautious. “Sweetheart, I–”

“I have to go!” Agatha said, reaching for the door and running out before the principal could say another thing to her.

Chapter 14: Witch with a Capital B

Chapter Summary

Agatha realizes something Very Bad

Chapter Notes

But here is the answer! Enjoy this lead in to the next chapter which should be out within the next week-ish.

Chapter Fourteen

Agatha was on edge. 

As she’d put her room back together - including gluing and taping back together the things her mother had broken, as well as tossing a few unsalvageable things - she catalogued every single time in her room, trying to find anything that might have been missing. 

All of her posters from the different shows she’d seen and wanted to see were still in her rooms, though many in disarray due to being torn nearly in half by her mother’s hands. Agatha used some mending spells to put them back together, but there were defects at the seams from her unskilled magic. She’d had a little better luck putting together her broken lamp, though it still flickered when it was on for too long.

She’d catalogued each of her playbills from the various shows she’d seen, both locally and on Broadway (and off-Broadway) over the years. Some were bent or torn slightly, but none were missing. She didn’t know why her mother would have taken any of them, anyway. 

She made her bed with fresh sheets, using the forest green ones that reminded her of Rio and tucking her Valentine’s Day violet into her pillowcase. All of the flowers that Rio had given her recently were scattered around the vase on her desk, which was miraculously still intact. She tucked them all back in, running her fingers over the petals, both soft and brittle with decay. 

Luckily, her laptop had been with her at school that day, so her mother hadn’t had the chance to break it, either on purpose or by accident. The papers she’d kept in her desk drawers were all accounted for, as were all of her pens and papers. The sole picture she had of her and her mother (when Agatha was five years old, smiling for the camera at her birthday party with her mother smiling behind her, pretending to be a doting, loving mother) was cracked, but not fully demolished. Agatha laid it down somewhere unseen, anyway. 

All of her clothes were accounted for, and put them lovingly back into her closet and folded them into her dresser drawers. Her shoes were unharmed, as well. Her crochet needles and yarn had also been scattered, and Agatha spent a good deal of time untangling some knots, but they were also pretty okay. She had every skein of yarn she’d bought for herself tucked away into a storage box at the bottom of her closet in no time. 

But she couldn’t find what she was missing . Her mother had said that she didn’t find what she was looking for, but Agatha was almost certain that was a lie. Evanora lied as often as she breathed. In her darkest moments, Agatha wished that both would stop.

Once everything was righted in her bedroom, Agatha had focused on her homework (an AP Earth Science study that she had to complete as well as an essay on The Scarlet Letter for AP English that she hadn’t yet completed) and had fallen into bed around midnight, thoroughly exhausted.

She hadn’t even thought about the empty spot on the bulletin board above her desk until two days later, when Lilia slipped a note down on Agatha’s desk, requesting a meeting at lunch. Agatha had frowned at the note, looking up at Lilia during class. The older woman just shook her head even as she gave Agatha a deadly serious expression. 

So, this was serious. 

Agatha chanced a glance over her shoulder at Rio, whose eyebrows were lifted. She held up her own note from Lilia. Agatha’s eyes widened further.

This was very serious.

Agatha was stressed for the rest of the morning, her eyes practically glued to the clock as she waited for lunch. She still avoided Rio in the halls, for the most part, and sent glares to Harper (whose nose had gone back to normal, unfortunately), but every nerve in her body was anxious. 

She had never gotten this nervous, not even as a child during her first audition, but she felt like this was what stage fright was like for other people.

She was happy she’d never had to experience it before. And she would never experience it again.

When lunch rolled around, Agatha practically sprinted to Lilia’s classroom. Lilia was at the door to welcome her, ushering her into the room with a silent wave, then closing the door behind them.

Rio came through the window just a couple of moments later. Lilia didn’t even complain about her entrance, which unnerved Agatha just a little bit more.

“What’s going on?” she asked the teacher, practically bouncing on her feet. “Did something happen? Did my mother call you?”

“No,” Lilia said, but her voice was quiet as she leaned against her desk, “your mother did not call me. But…I did receive a call.” She took a deep breath. “From Juilliard.”

“Juilliard?” Agatha asked, frowning in confusion. “Why would Juilliard call you? And what does it have to do with me?”

“When is your audition with them?” Lilia asked.

“Um, next week,” Agatha said. “I don’t quite remember the exact day. I’d have to check my…letter.” Her eyes widened as the realization hit her. “No. No, no, no, no, no!” She closed her eyes, placing her hands over her face. “No! That bitch !” She opened her eyes and looked at Lilia, whose lips were tight. “What did they say?”

“They said ,” Lilia replied, knowingly, “that a parent had called to cancel your audition yesterday, that you were no longer interested in attending their school in the fall.”

Agatha felt her heart drop, felt her knees go weak, felt herself begin to fall…

Rio caught her, wrapping her arms around Agatha’s waist, holding her up, hugging her against Rio’s body as Agatha’s breathing went shallow. It was as if all the air had been sucked out of the room. Rio rubbed her back, rocking her a little bit in her arms, pressing kisses to her temple as she whispered words that Agatha couldn’t quite hear into her ear. They were soothing, nonetheless.

She felt another hand on her shoulder and looked up to see Lilia standing in front of her, hazel eyes warm and sympathetic. She pushed some of Agatha’s hair behind her ear.

“I told them,” she said, “that they were mistaken.”

“What?” Agatha asked, her head still cloudy with confusion - and probably the lack of oxygen she’d been taking in as the panic set over her.

“When they asked me what was going on there because you listed me as your main point of contact, not your mother, I told them that it must have been a mistake, that you were very much interested in continuing on with your audition next week.”

Agatha remembered putting down Lilia’s name on the application sheet. She remembered putting down Lilia’s contact information (just the school email and extension she had for Lilia at the time, hoping they would be enough) in place of her mother’s. She had prayed they wouldn’t ask for her actual Legal Guardian’s information…and they didn’t.

Lilia, as it happened, had some kind of sway at Juilliard. Probably due to the fact that she’d sent about a dozen award-winning actors through there after leaving Westview.

Agatha had never been more grateful for that than she was right at this moment.

She left Rio’s arms, throwing herself immediately into Lilia’s, pressing her face into Lilia’s shoulder as tears fell from her eyes, soaking into the collar of Lilia’s yellow blouse. The shorter woman rubbed Agatha’s back, her hold tight on her student, comforting her.

“Thank you,” Agatha warbled, pulling back, her chin quivering. “Thank you so much.”

“Don’t worry about it, doll,” Lilia said, holding her chin. “You just go and blow the roof off that place, okay?” Agatha nodded. “Now,” she continued, “we do need to talk about your mother.”

“My…why?” Agatha huffed, pulling away. “I can deal with her.”

“Agatha,” Lilia said, her voice low and warning. “That woman just literally tried to destroy your future. Why would she do that?”

“Because she’s a bitch,” Rio muttered. “With a capital ‘b’.”

“What Rio said,” Agatha agreed. “Look, it’s fine. She’s just…” Agatha’s eyes filled with tears. “I don’t know. But it’s fine. I’ll be fine.”

“No,” Lilia said, firmly, wrapping her fingers lightly around Agatha’s wrist, “I don’t think you will be fine. Not so long as that woman is controlling your life like this.” She twisted her lips into a frown. “Billy Kaplan told me that something happened the other day, at home.”

Agatha’s eyes widened. So did Rio’s.

She hadn’t told her about what her mother had done. She hadn’t wanted the other girl to be upset.

“Little shit,” Agatha huffed, looking down and kicking the ground lightly with her feet. “It’s not that big a deal,” she said, looking up between Lilia and Rio.

“Not a big…Agatha, that woman destroyed your bedroom . She tore up personal items, broke furniture .”

“She did what ?” Rio growled.

“She broke a lamp ,” Agatha said, rolling her eyes. “And it was easy enough to fix. It’s really not–”

“ Don’t say it’s not a big deal,” Rio said, her voice hard. “She tore up your room? For what ? To find your letter from Juilliard? The one pinned to your bulletin board above your desk?” She remembered seeing it the one and only time she’d stepped inside that house. It was in plain view. “Why did that require her breaking a lamp?”

“She just…has to be extra,” Agatha huffed. “I don’t know…”

“She was trying to distract you,” Lilia said, softly. “She knew you would be too preoccupied with fixing all the things she’d done in your room to notice that the letter was removed. And she was correct. Did you even realize that it was gone before I called you in here?” Agatha shook her head. “Your mother is a calculating bitch. I don’t know why she’s decided to do this, specifically, but she likely has something bigger planned. You need to get away from her as soon as possible.”

“And go where?” Agatha huffed. “She has a tracing spell on me, remember? If I’m not exactly where I’m supposed to be at all times, she will know. She’ll find me wherever I go. She needs to keep up appearances with her friends, so she needs to keep her control over me for as long as she possibly can. I have nowhere to run where she can’t find me.” Her breath hitched and she felt Rio’s arms wind around her from behind, her chin landing on Agatha’s shoulders as she went back to whispering soothing words into her ear. Agatha placed her hands over Rio’s, interlocking their fingers. 

“I think I may know how to rid you of the tracing spell entirely,” Lilia said.

Both Agatha and Rio gave her wide-eyed stares. “What?” Agatha asked. “You…you do ?”

“Maybe,” Lilia said. “I have a collection of books at home that may be able to help us. You don’t get to be as old as I am without picking up a few things, even things that aren’t necessarily in your own discipline. This falls under the purview of Protection magic, though, so we’ll need to find a protection witch to take point.”

Agatha pulled out of Rio’s hold and turned to look her in the eyes. They were both thinking the exact same thing: Alice .

Chapter End Notes

Chapter 15: Pentagram

Chapter Summary

Agatha gets ready to escape Evanora's influence. It's emotional and raw and she didn't really expect how it would make her feel. She also finds out that she has more people in her corner than she'd originally thought possible.

Chapter Notes

My period had me practically sobbing for this chapter. Y'all better appreciate it.

Also, we meet the moms. Yay!

Chapter Fifteen

Rio found Alice and Jen in the chorus room after school on Friday. There was no theater rehearsal (Lilia always gave them Fridays off until the last few weeks of rehearsals, when it became all hands on deck) but Alice was still preparing for her own Juilliard auditions, which would take place in March and were, honestly, just a formality. Jen was sitting on the bench next to Alice, leaning her head on the shorter girl’s shoulder while she tapped away an unfamiliar tune on the piano. It had a nice rhythm to it, though, Rio had to admit. 

“What song is that?” Rio asked, making both girls jump as the song came to a halt.

Alice and Jen turned to look at her, the former’s cheeks turning pink. “Oh,” she said. “It’s nothing. Just, um, a version of my mom’s song. The witch song, you know? Different lyrics, though.”

“I didn’t hear any lyrics,” Rio said, taking a seat on one of the risers set up in a semicircle in front of the piano. Her fingers twitched, nervously.

“I’m still working out the music,” Alice said. “Trying to get everything to fit before my audition.”

“What are you doing here?” Jen asked, raising one eyebrow, suspiciously.

Rio thought about lying, but that would only waste time in the long run. They didn’t have time to waste. 

“I need your help,” Rio said, looking directly at Alice, whose eyes widened. 

“ My help?” Alice asked, turning on the bench to face her. “With what? Your solos?”

“No,” Rio said, chuckling slightly. “I think you’ve actually done about as much as you can with what I have to work with.”

“Your voice is good,” Alice insisted, offering a genuine smile. Rio couldn’t help but return it. Alice acted all dark and moody – much like Rio had always acted after her father’s passing – but she was probably one of the sweetest people Rio had ever known. She was the daughter of a world-famous musician, but she never acted stuck-up or full of herself. Her smiles were always more or less genuine and filled with happiness, and she got genuinely happy when somebody nailed a solo. If she didn’t become a rockstar in her own right, Rio could see her becoming a kickass teacher, just like Lilia.

“Thanks,” Rio said, clearing her throat. “But, um, really, that’s not why I came.”

“Then why did you come here, lurker?” Jen asked, lifting one brow.

“Really, Jen,” Rio huffed, “I figured we were past that nickname.”

“Not when you’re sitting there being all… lurky .” Jen motioned at Rio’s spot on the risers. “Still not being direct about why you’re disrupting my girlfriend’s practice. Now spill .”

“Agatha needs your help,” Rio blurted. She immediately pressed her lips together at the looks of surprise on both girls’ faces. She looked down, clearing her throat. “Her mother…”

“Is a bitch?” Jen finished for her. “We’re aware. Carry on.”

“Yeah, she is. She’s a bitch that just tried to cancel Agatha’s Juilliard audition.”

“What the fuck ?!” Jen exclaimed, getting to her feet. Her hands immediately began to glow pink with her magic, rage radiating from her. “What a fucking psychopath !”

“Agreed,” Rio said. “That’s exactly how I felt about it when I found out. Especially since she also destroyed Agatha’s bedroom.”

“She what?” Alice’s eyes widened in worry, but there was also anger there. “Why would she do that?”

“Because she’s a bitch, babe,” Jen retorted. “Haven’t you heard?” The pink glow in her hands intensified for a moment, then diminished as she looked back at Rio. “What can Alice do about it? What do you need her help for?”

“Agatha’s mom put a trace on her,” Rio said. “We need to get rid of it.”

“Um…okay?” Alice said, tilting her head in confusion. “ I don’t know how to remove a trace.”

“But you’re a protection witch, aren’t you?”

“Yeah…?”

“And your mother is one, too?”

“Um, yeah,” Alice said. “So?”

“Lilia has a spell that can remove traces,” Rio said. “But we need a Protection Witch to take point on it.”

“Oh,” Alice said, her cheeks going pink. “I don’t think my magic is strong enough…”

“I kind of figured,” Rio said. “No offense. My green magic isn’t even strong enough to heal a minor headache.” She shrugged one shoulder with a self-deprecating smile. “But would your mother…would she be willing to help with this?”

Lorna Wu was a rockstar, but she’d always been nice. She was the kind of mom who brought cupcakes in at lunch for her daughter’s birthday. She attended all their shows and even paid extra for the “Angel seats” in the front row center, inviting other show moms who otherwise couldn’t have afforded it. She took pictures and donated money to the music and theater departments at the school. She started scholarships for performing arts students and hosted the post-show parties at her home. She absolutely adored her daughter and would do anything for her. Including making sure that her touring schedule never interfered with Alice’s theatre schedule, even though Alice wasn’t even on the stage. 

Lorna Wu was the kind of mom that Agatha deserved. So did Alice, obviously. Lorna had obviously raised a decent person in her.

“Is she hurting Agatha?” Alice asked. Rio clenched her jaw as she nodded. “Then, yes, I can almost guarantee you that my mom would be willing to help in any way that she can. Do you have the spell?”

Rio reached into her pocket and pulled out the photocopied spell. She handed it to Alice, who unfolded the paper and skimmed it, her eyes widening. 

“This is complex,” she said. “It needs a lot. Um, when do we have to get this done? Is there a deadline?”

“Well,” Rio said, “considering Agatha’s mom tried to cancel her audition, which is happening next week, I think getting it done sooner rather than later is ideal. Your mom’s not on tour, is she?”

“She only tours during the summer,” Alice said. “She’s been recording a new album and doing local shows for the most part in preparation, but she’ll be around all week.” She furrowed her brow. “Agatha can’t come to my house, can she?”

“Probably not,” Rio said. “Her mom usually doesn’t care where she goes between school and home, but, lately, Agatha said that she’s been acting…strangely. My guess is that she’s going to try to trap Agatha at home during her audition, so she’s watching her much more closely for a while.”

“Why would she do this to her own daughter?” Alice said, her voice soft and eyes wet. “I know you said she’s a bitch, but…that’s really not enough of an answer, you know? Juilliard is a really good school. Anybody else would be bragging that their daughter got in. Why sabotage it?”

“Because she wants Agatha to be as miserable as she is,” Jen huffed, crossing her arms over her chest. “And that means taking away her dreams.” She shook her head, her hands starting to glow again.

Alice reached for her, wrapping her arms around Jen’s waist, pressing a kiss to her cheek. Rio could swear she saw a tear form in the corner of Jen’s eyes, but she blinked it away as she held Alice back. Rio didn’t point it out, either.

“Thanks,” she said, quietly. “Um, if you can, maybe on Monday or Tuesday we can perform…whatever it is we need to do.”

Alice nodded. “Yeah,” she said. “I think my mom has most of this stuff. But, uh, we need more witches, too.”

“What kind of witches?” Rio asked, grimacing. The only witches she knew, besides her own mother, were teenagers and probably didn’t have the necessary magic skill for a spell of this magnitude, even combined with other unskilled witches.

“A Green witch,” Alice said, looking down at the paper Rio had handed her. “A Divination witch – we already have Lilia. And a Potions witch.” She looked at Jen. “Your mom is Potions, too, right?”

“Yeah,” Jen said. “She can help.” She looked at Rio with a nod.

“Thanks,” Rio said. “My mom is a Green witch, too. I’m sure it will take her no convincing, especially if she gets to meet my girlfriend in the process.”

“I’ll talk to my mom tonight about it,” Jen said.

“Me too,” Rio said.

“Me too,” Alice added. “I’m sure she’ll say yes.” She pulled out her phone and handed it over to Rio. “Give me your number,” she said. “I’ll text you tonight to let you know.”

Rio nodded, quickly taking the phone and tapping in her contact info, then taking a selfie of herself flipping off the camera for her contact photo. Jen rolled her eyes and Alice snorted. Rio handed the phone back with a shrug.

Alice sent her a text and Rio immediately saved her contact before raising her camera to take Alice’s picture, waving Jen away as she attempted to get in on it. Jen flipped her off but stepped out of frame as Alice smiled at the camera.

“Boring,” Rio huffed even as she took the picture. “You’re a rockstar’s daughter, Wu! C’mon!”

“It’s Wu- Gulliver , actually,” Alice said. “My dad’s a cop.

“Boo,” Rio said. Jen echoed her, receiving a pout from Alice in response.

“Your dad is chill,” Jen said, “but you know how I feel about the police.” She shrugged, unapologetically.

“Fair,” Rio said. Alice frowned at her next. “I said what I said.”

“Whatever,” Alice huffed. “Can I get back to my song now? Or would you two like to make even more digs about my father’s profession?”

“Is that an option?” Rio asked, mockingly excited.

“No,” Alice said, glowering.

“Then, I’ll just leave you to it,” Rio said, giving her a mock salute. “Thanks, Alice. Really.”

“No problem,” Alice sighed. “Um, tell Agatha…I’m sorry about her mom. I wish I could do more to help.”

Rio paused at that, turning back to them. “Um, actually,” she said, “you can.”

 

Agatha spent most of her weekend packing her things away into the enchanted bag Lilia had given her. She knew that the second the tracing spell was removed, her mother would know and she wouldn’t be able to return to the home she’d known all of her life. Living with her mother would be impossible, so she was finally taking the plunge and moving out.

To where, she had yet to figure out.

Billy had spoken to his parents in the hypothetical about them possibly taking in a new foster kid. He and his brother each had their own room at their house, and they also had a spare room. His mother, he said, was not against the idea. Still, it was only hypothetical.

Lilia was another option, but Agatha didn’t want to get her in trouble with the school for inviting a student to live in her spare room. Rio was yet another option, but…well, it would be awkward. They’d only been dating about a month and Agath still hadn’t even met her mother. Besides, she didn’t know if they’d even have room for her.

She tried to shoo the thoughts from her mind as she packed up the bag, which was bigger on the inside. She put as much inside it as she dared, not wanting to raise suspicion from her mother. As far as Evanora knew, she was just clearing up some clutter in her room. She barely even noticed that anything had been taken out, anyway.

She only really packed what was most important to her, anyway. She could get a new bedframe, a new desk, new end tables, etc. She brought the majority of her clothes, leaving only the ugly sweaters and dresses that she’d been gifted from her mothers’ friends hanging up in her closet. She packed up her crochet stuff, her playbills, and her posters. She emptied her desk drawers and packed away her favorite bedsheets and comforters, replacing them on her bed with the generic floral ones her mom had bought for the guest room. She left her desk bare except for that cracked picture frame she had of them in their happy facade when she was five years old. She packed away everything that meant anything real to her, leaving anything that her mother had given her behind. 

She had used part of her savings to buy a new phone after school on the previous Friday. She filled the contacts from her old phone but made sure to block her mother’s number in case she somehow got the new number. She made her emergency medical contact Lilia. She texted hearts to Rio – as many as she’d always wanted to – and added both Jen’s and Alice’s numbers to her phone, letting them know where they could reach her. 

She packed up her whole life, changed everything, in preparation for the freedom she was about to have.

And then she sat on her bed and cried, keeping herself as silent as possible so as not to alert her mother. Evanora wouldn’t be worried about Agatha. She wouldn’t attempt to comfort her or ask her why she was crying out of genuine concern for her daughter. No, of course not.

She would be annoyed at Agatha for showing weakness. She would shame her and tell her to grow up, that she was far too old to be crying alone in her bedroom. She would probably even mock her.

And she would be suspicious. Agatha didn’t need her to be suspicious.

Agatha gave herself ten minutes to cry it all out, to be sad that she was leaving her home, that her mother’s actions were forcing her to find refuge elsewhere. She cried for the younger version of herself who deserved a loving and attentive mother, for the current version of herself who deserved a supportive mother. Not just supportive of her sexuality, but of her intended future.

She cried for the future children she might have, who would never know Evanora – not if Agatha could help it. She would never allow her mother to make another child feel the way she’d made Agatha feel. She’d love her kids the way her mother had never loved her. She’d treat them with the love and respect and kindness that she was never afforded. She imagined children with brown eyes and little gaps in their teeth, whose little hands glowed green with their own magic.

Then she shook those thoughts off. They were dangerous thoughts to have. She was seventeen years old, after all. It was far too soon…

Still, she let the thought fill her with warmth as she laid down in her bed, fully clothed on top of the covers, and dreamed about the future she might have some day, far away from her mother.

 

The bag fit perfectly in her backpack despite all it was carrying. Agatha had to remember to ask Lilia about what spell she’d placed on it or where she’d gotten the enchanted item later, after she had broken the trace and unpacked in her new home – wherever that may be.

Evanora didn’t suspect a thing, and Agatha chalked that up to her years of theater training. She made herself toast for breakfast, burning it a little bit as she scrolled on her old phone, trying not to raise her mother’s suspicions. Evanora read the paper and drank coffee, not even bothering to acknowledge Agatha’s existence in any way. Agatha fought the urge to roll her eyes as she buttered her toast and filled up her water bottle at the kitchen sink before walking out into the chilly February morning.

Her walk to school was uneventful as she munched on her toast between sips of water. It was starting to get warmer in New Jersey. Most of the snow had melted, leaving only small traces on the grass. Agatha didn’t have to wind her scarf quite as snugly to stay warm today. She was enjoying the warmth of the sun on her face. It felt like so long since she’d felt it last.

Okay, that was a bit dramatic, but still. New Jersey was cold as hell in the winter.

When she got to the school, she sent a text to Rio to let her know that she had arrived. Rio sent back a picture of a selfie she’d taken in the dressing room. Jen and Alice were there, too. They were posing in the mirror like a bunch of dorks. Agatha snorted at the picture, shaking her head as she tucked her phone away into her pocket.

They had twenty minutes until the first bell when Agatha entered the dressing room, and Lilia’s classroom was just up a short staircase outside the auditorium. Rio was sitting with her back to the mirror while Alice and Jen were entangled as they scrolled their phones against the adjacent wall. Agatha locked the door as she stepped inside.

“Hey,” Rio said, looking up at her. Agatha offered her a tired smile as she dramatically collapsed next to Rio, laying her head down in Rio’s lap.

“Hey,” she said in return, accepting the kiss that Rio gave her, leaning down to press their lips to Agatha’s.

“Gross,” Jen huffed, wrinkling her nose.

“Kale, I just watched you and Alice suck face for, like, five minutes without taking a single breath. Shut up.”

Alice flushed bright red, pressing her face into Jen’s shoulder while the other girl flipped Rio off with both hands. Rio scoffed, looking down at Agatha as she ran her fingers through her hair.

“How was your weekend?” Rio asked.

“Quiet,” Agatha sighed. “I stayed in my room for most of Saturday. On Sunday, I went to church, but I acted sick throughout the service and faked vomiting to get out of Bible study. Mom couldn’t resist acting like the doting mother in front of her friends, but she let me have it when she got home that night even though I was still acting sick. Apparently I embarrassed her.” Agatha snorted. “Good.” She sat up, looking over at Jen and Alice. “So,” she said, “what did your moms say about the spell?”

“My mom’s in,” Alice said. “Of course. She has no show tonight and she has, like, 80% of the things we need for it.”

“My mom is also in,” Jen said. “She has the other 20%.”

“My mom’s coming, too,” Rio said. “She would like to formally introduce herself before this whole thing starts, though,” she added to Agatha, who blushed.

“I would be very okay with that,” Agatha said, smiling. “I want to meet her, too.” She cleared her throat, blushing at the very soft look Rio was giving her. “So,” she said, “when are we doing this? I packed up all my stuff already. It will probably take my mom a couple of days to notice.”

“My mom’s ready tonight,” Alice said. “If that’s not too soon, I mean.”

“So is my mom,” Jen said. “She is so ready to stick it to your mom. She’s always hated her.”

“She can join the club then,” Agatha snorted. “What about your mom, Ri?”

“She gets home at three, so as long as we do it then, I’m sure she’ll be here. You really wanna do it today? Are you sure you’re ready for that?”

“If I don’t do it soon, she might try to physically prevent me from going to my audition on Saturday,” Agatha said. “I…I don’t think I can go back to that house. I can’t put myself through that anymore, Rio. I just…even if I end up sleeping on the street tonight, I can’t go back there.”

“You’ll never sleep on the street,” Rio said, frowning. She cupped Agatha’s cheeks, forcing her to look her in the eyes. “No matter what, you’ll always have a place to go. You just have to let people help you.”

“I hate it,” Agatha said, her chin quivering. A lump formed in her throat.

“Hate what?” Alice asked.

“Relying on other people,” Agatha admitted. 

The second the words left her lips, it was as if a weight had been lifted from her shoulders. She had never admitted that aloud to anybody else before. She had never really even voiced it out loud at all . And now she had just done that in a room with three other people – two of whom she wasn’t quite as close to as the third. 

But here they all were, planning to help her escape from her abusive mother. They had roped in their own mothers to help her. She quite literally couldn’t do this without them. And that scared the hell out of her. Because the one person she was supposed to be able to rely on was now forcing her into this very situation. How was she really supposed to rely on others now, after that?

Well, she was about to find out.

 

Lorna Wu was instantly recognizable, no matter how hard she tried. And she tried. Hard .

She wore an oversized hoodie, sunglasses, and a ballcap as she entered the school, her hands tucked into her pockets and her shoulders slumped as if she were trying to blend in with the rest of the student population. But her long, silky black hair gave her away almost immediately as it emerged from her hood. Whispers followed her through the halls as she made her way from the front door to the auditorium, a designer backpack on her back.

She offered subtle smiles to teens who stopped to gawk at her, but she didn’t stop. She was a woman on a mission. 

Alice met her at the door to the auditorium, and Lorna immediately embraced her in a tight hug, practically crushing her daughter in her arms. Alice pressed her face into her mother’s shoulder. Lorna Wu was statuesque, standing at nearly six feet tall even without heels, while Alice was barely five foot one. She must have gotten her height from her father, who was distinctly shorter than her mom.

But she didn’t seem to mind.

“Hello, baby girl,” Lorna said, pulling away to cup her daughter’s cheeks. “How was school today?”

“It was good,” Alice said, rolling her eyes. “As always. Did you bring the stuff?”

“Got it right here,” Lorna said, patting her backpack. “Is Naomi here yet?”

“She should be here soon,” Alice informed her. “Jen’s been texting with her.”

“Okay,” Lorna said. “So where are we setting up? And who is our green witch, exactly?”

“Lilia is letting us use her classroom,” Alice said. “It’s right upstairs. Rio Vidal’s mom is a Green witch. She should be here soon, too.”

“Okay…who’s Rio Vidal?” Lorna asked, even as Alice tugged her along toward the stairs to the balcony. 

“Keep up, Ma,” Alice snorted. “I sent you everything in a text.”

“Sweetie, you have no idea how to separate your paragraphs,” Lorna huffed. “I can’t just read a whole wall of text like that, come on!”

Alice laughed and rolled her eyes, guiding Lorna up the stairs and through the door in the balcony to Lilia’s classroom. All the desks had been pushed against the walls. All the windows were covered so that nobody could see inside. There was a chalk drawing of a pentagram in the middle of the floor. Agatha and Rio were sitting on the counter, and Lilia was at her desk, flipping through her spellbook. George was sitting with her, perched in a chair across from her. His eyes widened when Lorna and Alice stepped inside. He stood, clapping his hands. 

“Oh my!” he exclaimed. “Lorna Wu!” He stepped forward, holding out his hand. “My dear, it has been ages !”

“George!” Lorna exclaimed, pulling him into a hug. “It’s only been a year! Don’t be so dramatic!” She giggled. “How’s Manuelo?”

“Ah, you remembered!” George said, smiling as he pulled back. “He’s in Barcelona, teaching a class. He’ll be here in time for the show, or so he says.”

“Oh, he will!” Lorna snorted. “You know he will. I’ll save him a spot in the first row.”

“Of course you will!” George laughed. “An angel you are.” He cupped her cheeks. “You raised a very talented young woman, too. She is a virtuoso! ”

“Don’t I know it,” Lorna said, looking down at Alice, who blushed, hunching her shoulders as if to hide her face. “So, where do I set up?”

“In the pentagram,” Lilia snorted. “Where else?”

“Just checking,” Lorna said, slipping off her backpack and setting it on the ground next to the pentagram.

Agatha and Rio watched as she set down a few objects on the floor – they were stones, for the most part, and bundles of different herbs. Agatha recognized sage, at least. And bay leaves. And mint. Lorna laid each item down with purpose, murmuring words under her breath that Agatha couldn’t quite make out.

Rio squeezed her hand, diverting her attention.

“My mom’s here,” she whispered. “Do you wanna come down and meet her with me?”

“I don’t know if that’s a good idea…” Agatha said, looking around. “Somebody could see…”

“Agatha, we’re about to sever your ties to your mother,” Rio reminded her. “Does it really matter at this point?”

Agatha wanted to relent, but she knew that as long as there was a chance of this getting back to Evanora in some way, shape or form, she still wasn’t safe. Not until after the connection was broken. She wanted desperately to let go of that fear and throw caution to the window, but…

“I can’t,” Agatha said, tugging her hand from Rio’s grasp. “I…it’s not safe. Not yet. Harper or one of her friends might still be here. If they see us…”

Rio nodded in understanding, but Agatha could see the disappointment in her eyes. It made her want to scream, to take back her words, to just go with her.

But she didn’t. Instead, she watched Rio cross the room and go through the door to the balcony. Alone.

“This better fucking work,” she muttered, kicking her feet.

“It will,” Jen said, coming into the room with her own mother, Naomi, in tow.

Naomi was a couple inches shorter than Jen, but no less fierce and confident. She wore a lavender pantsuit and her long dark hair was in twists that cascaded down her back and over her shoulders. She had sunglasses perched on top of her head and a fur coat over her arm. She also had Jen’s Resting Bitch Face, but that soon split into a smile when Lorna stood and greeted her with a hug and a kiss to the cheek.

“Naomi!” she exclaimed. “How’s my future sister-in-law?”

“I don’t think that’s how that works, Mom,” Alice murmured at her side.

“Of course it is!” Lorna huffed, smiling as Naomi grabbed Alice next, placing a kiss in the center of the teen’s forehead and pinching her cheek.

“I’m well, thanks,” Naomi said. “Alice, look at you. It’s been forever since Jen brought you over for dinner.” She lightly smacked her daughter’s shoulder. “What is wrong with you!?”

“Hey!” Jen protested. “It’s not my fault you’ve been working late, Miss Civil Rights Attorney! Jeez!” She pouted and Naomi gently pinched her nose. “Mo-om!” Jen groaned, knocking her hand away.

“You two should come over this weekend,” Naomi said. “Sunday dinner? We can celebrate Jenny’s immediate entrance into Juilliard after they see her audition!”

“Ugh,” Jen huffed, blushing. “I don’t think it happens that quickly.”

“It will for you, baby girl,” Naomi cooed, holding Jen’s chin between her fingers. “Mark my words.” She pressed another kiss to her cheek, then reached into the purse that had been hidden underneath her fur coat, pulling out a bowl made of jade. “This is what you needed, right?”

“Precisely!” Lorna said, joyfully taking the bowl. “And a little eye of newt.”

“Also known as mustard seed,” Naomis said, taking out a little spice jar and handing it over.

She then turned in Agatha’s direction, causing the teen girl to flush and hunch her shoulders as Naomi Kale’s gaze zeroed in on her. The woman stalked forward, her brows furrowed. Agatha’s fists clenched around the material of her sweater. Naomi stopped just inches from her, her eyes scanning over Agatha’s entire body, sizing her up.

Then, she did the most shocking thing Agatha could possibly think of: she wrapped her arms around the teen girl’s shoulders, pulling her into a tight hug. Agatha’s eyes widened as her face pressed against Naomi’s shoulder, tears immediately springing to her eyes. She hesitantly raised her arms, wrapping them around Naomi’s waist in response. She felt Naomi’s hand rub up and down her back for a moment before the woman pulled away, looking down into her eyes.

“I’m so sorry,” Naomi said, softly, “for everything you’ve been through. I wish I could have helped you. I really do.”

Agatha nodded, fighting back the tears again. Damn, she had been so close to losing it far too much that day. She hated this.

“It’s okay,” she said. “You’re helping now. That’s all that matters.”

Naomi nodded, stroking Agatha’s hair. She placed a kiss on her forehead. “Jennifer said that you needed a place to go,” she said as she pulled back. “You can stay with us tonight, if you want.”

Agatha’s eyes widened. “Really?” she asked. Naomi Kale was the last person Agatha would have expected to extend an invite to Agatha into her home. She would have thought Naomi would have held a grudge against her for what happened all those years ago. “You…you don’t have to do that,” she said, glancing at Jen, who seemed to be intentionally avoiding her gaze. “I know…I know I’m not exactly your favorite person. Or Jen’s.”

“Do you think I don’t know that what happened between you and Jennifer wasn’t the direct influence of your mother?” Naomi scoffed. “ Please . If your mother hadn’t found out that Jenny was gay, she would have found some other reason to make you stop hanging out with her. She’s just a hater. And you deserve so much better.” She stroked Agatha’s hair again. “The invitation stands, sweetheart. If you need a place to lay your head for a few nights, you’re more than welcome at the Kale residence, okay?” Agatha nodded, offering her a grateful smile, still fighting back tears. Naomi squeezed Agatha’s shoulder with another smile before turning back to Lorna. “Okay,” she said, “now where do you need me?”

“Right over here,” Lorna said, pointing to one of the star’s points. “I need you to start grinding some of the ingredients together.”

“That I can do,” Naomi said, tossing her coat onto one of the desks and sitting down next to Lorna on the ground. Lorna immediately started handing her ingredients.

Agatha watched them for a moment, trying to blink away the tears as she waited for Rio to return with…

“Is that her?” an unfamiliar voice asked. Agatha’s attention turned immediately to the door, her jaw dropping at the sight of Rio and her doppelganger mother. The only major difference between Rio and Marisol Vidal was the brightness of the latter’s clothes and the absence of dark makeup on her face. Otherwise, she was just a slightly older version of her daughter.

And she was looking straight at Rio with those familiar brown eyes.

“That’s her,” Rio said, smiling softly in Agatha’s direction. “Mami,” she said, “meet Agatha Harkness. Agatha, this is my mother.” They walked forward together, almost eerily in sync, as Agatha hopped off the counter.

“It’s nice to meet you, Mrs. Vidal,” she said, holding out her hand.

“ Please ,” the older woman scoffed, “call me Marisol. None of that Mrs. Vidal nonsense! Mrs. Vidal is my mother-in-law, God rest her soul.” She crossed herself and Agatha chuckled. Then Marisol pulled her into what was certainly a bone-crushing hug. “I am so happy to finally meet you!” she exclaimed. “Rio has told me so much about you.” She pulled back, holding Agatha by the shoulders. “ Ella realmente es tan bonita como dijiste .”

Both Rio and Agatha flushed bright pink. Agatha cleared her throat. “ Gracias, Señ-ahem, Marisol ,” Agatha said. 

“And she speaks Spanish?” she said, offering a thumbs up that she may have imagined was subtle to Rio. Rio facepalmed.

“Not fluently,” Agatha admitted. “But I’m getting an A in the class. Latin, too.”

“¡ Y ella es muy inteligente! ¡Muy bien! ”

“Mami!” Rio groaned, covering her face as Alice and Jen giggled. “Shut it!” she hissed at them.

“Ah!” Marisol admonished, finally releasing Agatha to turn on her daughter. “ No sea grosera ,” she said. “ Disculpe. ”

“Sorry,” Rio grumbled, rolling her eyes. 

“Excuse her, please,” Marisol huffed. Then she looked toward the other two grown women sitting on the floor. “Where do you need me, ladies?” she asked.

Lorna motioned to another point on the star and Marisol took her seat there. 

“Can you grow vervain?” Lorna asked. “It needs to be very fresh.”

“Of course,” Marisol said, holding out her hand. The plant grew and bloomed in her hand immediately. “How much do you need?”

“That should be enough,” Lorna said. “Thank you.” She took the plant that Marisol offered. “Ooh, this is a fine specimen. Good job! I’m Lorna, by the way. Lorna Wu.”

“Lorna Wu?” Marisol gasped. “Like the singer?” Lorna nodded, blushing a bit. “Oh, I love your music. Especially that witchy song. About the road? It’s so catchy!”

“Thank you so much!” Lorna said. “Always nice to meet an appreciator of music.”

“My name is Marisol, by the way,” Marisol said. “Marisol Vidal. I’m Rio’s mother.”

“It’s nice to meet you, Marisol,” Lorna said, shaking her hand.

“I’m Naomi Kale,” Naomi said, shaking Marisol’s hand next. “Jennifer’s mother.”

“Which one is Jennifer?” Marisol asked. Naomi motioned to Jen, who was seated next to Alice, the both of them perched on top of the desks against the wall. “Ooh,” Marisol said, “she is very pretty. And that’s your daughter next to her?” she asked Lorna, who nodded. “Also quite beautiful.”

“Thank you,” Lorna said, smiling. “Rio looks like a lovely girl, as well.”

“She is ,” Marisol responded, proudly. “And so very smart. I’m certain your girls are, as well?”

“Yes,” Lorna said. 

“Very smart,” Naomi added.

“Okay, can we form this book club later?” Jennifer huffed. “We need to get to rehearsals soon.”

“Patience, baby,” Naomi said. “Miss Calderu, are you going to join us down here?”

Lilia looked up from her spell book and nodded. “I’m coming,” she said. “I’m just trying to avoid sitting on the hard floor for as long as possible. The aches and pains it’s going to cause will be legendary.”

“So dramatic,” George scoffed.

“Well, I am a drama teacher, doll,” Lilia said, standing and shuffling over to the star. “Where do you want me?” she asked Lorna. Lorna pointed to the point next to Marisol. Lilia nodded and sat down, groaning a little as her ass met the cold, hard ground. “I hope this doesn’t take too long,” she said. “I can already feel my back protesting.”

“Once we get started,” Lorna said, “it should only take about five minutes…if everything goes to plan.”

“And if everything doesn’t go to plan?” Agatha asked, walking cautiously toward the circle.

Lorna pressed her lips together and shrugged. “I’m sure everything will be fine,” she said. “Come, sit.” She patted the last point on the Pentagram, right next to herself. “Girls, you too. We’ll need as much power as we can get for this one.”

“Is it going to hurt?” Agatha asked, even as she sat down.
